I. URBAN TRANSPORT IN THE RIO DE JANEIRO METROPOLITAN REGION
A. Background
1.1          This project has its origins in two major developments at the federal and state level: (a)
the decentralization of the Rio de Janeiro Urban Trains from the Federal Government to the State of Rio
de Janeiro which took place in December 1994 (as part of an ongoing Bank loan 3633-BR); and (b) the
decision of the new State administration which took office in January 1995 to achieve a more effective
integration, of all modes in the Rio de Janeiro Metropolitan Region (RJMR), with an aim of reducing
subsidies/to urban transport agencies and substantially increasing the participation of the private sector
in the operation and investment of the sector.
1.2          The State decided to embark on a program of staff rationalization and restructuring of its
urban transportation agencies as part of a State Reform Project. To consolidate the gains made in the
decentralization project and substantially increase private sector participation in its metropolitan rail
agency (Flumitrens), the State requested the assistance of the Bank to complete the rehabilitation of
Flumitrens and the integration with other modes. The identification mission took place in November
1995, and based on its findings, the Bank agreed to proceed with the evaluation of the project provided
the State of Rio de Janeiro (SRJ) would incorporate into its urban transport strategy the following two
elements: (a) effective coordination between the state and municipal governments of the Rio de Janeiro
Metropolitan Region (RJMR); and (b) a formal financing mechanisms considered crucial to create an
enabling environment for the long-term financial sustainability of the Rio de Janeiro Metropolitan
Region's urban transport systems; and (c) concession, sale or liquidation of all its urban transport
agencies. The SRJ moved swiftly and diligently to include these elements in its strategy.
B. Urban Transport in the RJMR: An Overview
1.3          The Rio de Janeiro  Metropolitan

3 percent by subway (Metro), 1 percent by ferry (CONERJ), and .5 percent by other modes. Of the 8.7
million trips by public modes, about half use more than one mode, requiring some sort of modal transfer:
37 percent of all Metro trips, 47 percent of all train trips, and 41 percent of all bus trips require one or
more transfers to be completed.
1.4          This level of urban transport activity, dominated by the road-based motorized modes,
has significant impacts on the RJMR's environment. Despite an existing 264-km rail network, the lack of
integration between the Metro (only 23 km) and the Flumitrens discourages more rail trips, in favor of
buses and the automobile, creating heavy congestion during peak hours and significantly increasing
home-to-work trip time. Rio's geography and topography with its hills and tunnels is prone to traffic
congestion, especially when accidents occur and/or cars break down. There is limited space for fully
segregated busways. It is estimated that each "carioca" (citizen of RJ) wastes 10-15 days a year more



- 2 -
than it should due to high traffic congestion. The main users of the public transport system are from the
Baixada Fluminense (a poor section of Rio) and bear the brunt of the system's problems: (a) shortage of
capacity at peak hours, resulting in overcrowded (>10 passlm2), (b) long work joumeys (2.5-4.0
hours/day for a round trip) from the metropolitan periphery to thie urban centers, with often more than two
modal transfers; and (c) paying over a fourth of their income towards fares. Lack of coordination between
the three levels of government responsible for urban transport, particularly between the SRJ and the
RJM, has led to poor tariff and modal integration, lack of prioritization in urban transport investments,
and no common policy on pricing and subsidies.
1.5          A Bank-financed Mass Transportation Plan (PTM, see Annex 13, Ref. 1) was completed
in 1995 and selected the main urban transport corridors of the RJMR as the area most in need of
rehabiilitation or new investments. This selection of corridors was based on comprehensive traffic survey
data and a demand modeling exercise with a modal split analysis comparing the several modal
alternatives for each corridor. The top-ranked group of corridors in terms of economic efficiency for the
region was the one formed by the Flumitrens metropolitan rail system, which could provide rail transport
services for many of the current road-based urban trips thereby decreasing bus congestion and
improving trip times for the users provided Flumitrens can provide an acceptable level of service in
terms of frequency and reliability. Alternative options such as busways were not considered feasible
given the volumes of passengers involved and the amount of expropriation and resettlement that would
be required. To achieve the level of service required, the PTM recommended that the Flumitrens system
be further rehabilitated and modernized to be able to carry a daily passenger load of 1.4 million
passengers by year 2002. The PTM also recommended a regional transport coordination commission
with representation from the State, Rio de Janeiro Municipality, and other municipalities of the RJMR,
users and operators be formally established to serve as a forurrm for the discussion and recommendation
of nevi major investments and common pricing and subsidy policies. Furthermore, the PTM emphasized
the need to better integrate the predominantly bus-oriented RJMR system with Flumitrens, the Metr6 and
the ferry. The Bank project team reviewed the reports prepared under the PTM and endorsed the results
of the! alternative analysis as well as the proposal for the establishment of a regional transport
coordination commission and a more integrated urban transport system. Finally, as recommended by the
PTM, the State should put in place the necessary financing mechanisms to ensure the sustainability of
the sector in the long run and prevent the deterioration caused by deferred maintenance. The above
recommendations agree with earlier Bank's departmental Economic Sector Work (Annex 13, ref. 19)
which set the foundations for the ongoing process of decentralization of railway systems from the Federal
to the State government.
1.6           Although the project team agreed with the recommendation of the PTM that Flumitrens
infrastructure and equipment needed to be rehabilitated, the main concern was to ensure that the system
be managed with a private sector orientation and would be able to overcome the problems of
mismanagement and inadequacy of maintenance budget which are common to most state-run railways
world-wide. Based on the conclusions of studies (financed under Ln. 3633-BR) on options for private
sector participation in Flumitrens, the project team organized several brainstorming meetings with the
key State decision-makers, and the result of this exercise was an agreement that the best option to
ensure an adequate level of service and the financial sustainability of the system would be a full
concession to the private sector of its operations and maintenance for a period of not less than ten years.
The second best alternative would be the concessioning out of some key railway activities such as rolling
stock maintenance and track infrastructure to the private sector while the system would be still run by a
state-appointed administration. In all cases, however, there was a consensus that after the completion of
the ongoing Ln. 3633-BR which supported the decentralization of the Flumitrens system from the Federal
Government to the State, there would still be a need for large-scale rehabilitation of the remaining rolling
stock because the lack of maintenance funds prior to decentralization of the system led to the
immobilization of almost 50 percent of the fleet. Based on the few similar experiences in the world and
since these type of concessions are normally negative (i.e., with a subsidy at least in the first 5 years),
the private sector is only interested in bidding if the bid for the concession includes a State-financed
investment plan. The project team also agreed that, since the process of concession which started with
the State Reform Loan would take at least one year and a half, it was necessary to allow Flumitrens to
start its investment plan prior to concession since its fleet availability was very low despite the cars
rehabilitated under Ln. 3633-BR. The risk of starting the rehabilitation prior to concession would be that
the concession may not materialize and a number of contracts would have been signed. However,



-3 -
without the inclusion of a state-financed investment plan in the bidding document-. the concession of the
system would be less attractive to potential concessionaires, and the present state government, which
has been advocating a comprehensive urban sector reform, would have only one year left on its
mandate and a rail system in precarious position. Thus it was decided that the benefits of starting the
investment plan before the concession is signed outweigh the risks of not having a concession signed by
the agreed date.
1.7          Recent departmental Economic and Sector Work (Annex 13, ref. 19) concluded that
particulate air pollution in the northern part of the Rio Municipality and the Baixada Fluminense is serious
enough to cause significant health damages including premature mortality. Although these particulates
are mostly from industry, they are also due to transport emissions and solid waste buming. Data on
ground-level ozone is lacking but climatic conditions and emissions characteristics would suggest the
possibility of a serious ozone problem in Rio. Ozone (smog) is mainly due to transport emissions of NOx
and VOC (ozone precursors). Traffic congestion in tunnels make them unbearable due to poor
ventilation. In 1994 the number of vehicles registered in the RJMR was 1.7 million. Vehicular air pollution
has been somewhat mitigated by the use of alcohol. Vehicular accidents, on the other hand, have
become a major source of environmental risks. Congestion and overcrowding of the public transport
system are a source of envirunmental stress. Throughout the day and particularly at peak-hours noise
levels are well above the acceptable levels recommended by WHO.
1.8           After the liquidation of the only state-owned company (CTC), all bus services are
provided by private operators at one of the lowest fares in the country. The bus private operators do not
receive subsidies while Flumitrens (suburban trains), Metro (subway) and CONERJ (ferries) received
subsidies of respectively US$188 million, US$109 million and US$30 million in 1995. Annex 4 details
each of the urban transport agencies under state jurisdiction. The CTC was recently liquidated as part of
the State's move to privatize urban transport services. Bids for the concession of old CTC routes were
issued by June 30, 1997.Finally, RJMR needs to modernize its traffic engineering and control technology
to be able to cope with the present and growing number of road-based vehicles and introduce policies
which will discourage the rapidly growing use of automobiles for home-to-work trips.
1.9          The road network is radial and converges on the central business district. The
commercial speed of the buses in the reserved bus lanes is around 30 km/hr but drops to 20 km/hr or
less in the central business district. At peak period, most highway corridors are clogged with bumper to
bumper buses and automobiles. Bus services are generally of low speed and unreliable at peak hours
due to the congestion. Although they are privately operated, the municipal bus routes are allocated on
the basis of competitive bidding and regulated by the Secretary of Municipal Transport, a municipal
agency. The privately operated intermunicipal bus services are under the jurisdiction of the Secretary of
Transport of the State. Tariffs are set by those agencies and there are no subsidies paid to private bus
operators. In the Rio de Janeiro Municipality (RJM), there is a flat tariff which applies to all the
municipality and every trip. In the case of the intermunicipal services the tariffs are distance-based.
1.10         At present, Flumitrens, the State-owned suburban railway is partially integrated with 150
bus lines in 30 terminals, which generates about 47 percent of its demand without an integrated tariff.
Flumitrens is also integrated with the Metro in 3 stations of which the most important is D. Pedro II.
Flumitrens estimates that with more frequent services, increased train availability and reliability,
rehabilitation of 13 stations, 5 intermodal integration terminals, and a number of pedestrian crosswalks,
demand would increase substantially. For passengers who have their origins and destinations within
walking distance of the stations, Flumitrens offers the most comfortable and reliable mode of transport.
However, if the origins and destinations are beyond a reasonable walking distance, the lack of good
integration and the lack of stations at important demand generation centers, makes the use of the system
awkward and more expensive. This lack of sufficient integration between Flumitrens and buses
discourages more rail trips and encourages bus and automobile usage, thereby increasing the heavy
congestion during peak hours and significantly increasing home-to-work trip time. In its program to
decentralize the responsibility of operation of its metropolitan systems from the Federal Government to
the State (Ln. 3633-BR), a first attempt at improving integration was made, but it fell short of its goals
mainly because the train fleet available at the time the State took over the system was well below what
had been estimated at appraisal, due to a lack of promised Federal budget resources for routine and
periodic maintenance.



C. Institutional Responsibilities for Urban Transport in the RJMR
1.11         There are three main government bodies responsible for overseeing the urban transport
sector in the RJMR: (a) the State Secretary of Transport (SECTRAN) which regulates all the
intermunicipal bus services, rail-based and ferry-boat services of the region; (b) the Secretary of
Transport of the Municipality of Rio de Janeiro which regulastes municipal bus services and is also
responsible for traffic management and control in the RJM; and (c) the State Directorate for Roads
(Departmento de Estradas de Rodagem do Estado do Rio de Janeiro, DER-RJ) responsible for all the
state iroads in the SRJ. Urban transport responsibilities within any of the other municipalities of the RJMR
are under the jurisdiction of the Prefeitura of that municipalit,y. Intermunicipal services fall under the
jurisdiction of the State. At present, although the RJM and the other municipalities of the RJMR are the
main beneficiaries of Flumitrens' system, the Metr6, and ferry services, they do not have direct
contributions to either the capital or operating budgets of urban transport agencies. This is a situation
which must be addressed through formal financing mechanisms since it is only reasonable that they pay
their lair share in the provision of transport services which benefit the employers and users in their
territoiries.
1.12          Municipal bus operators have a clearing house (Camara de CompensaQao) for bus
tariffs, and they pay bus services according to a standard cost formula which they approve. In this
manner, they compensate bus companies which operate with tariffs below the estimated cost of the
formula. Intermunicipal bus services do not have a clearing house and do not receive a subsidy from the
government.
1.13          Since three levels of government have been formulating policies and regulating urban
transport without a formal coordinating arrangement, there has been a lack of consistency in the fares
chargEid by similar or competing modes, duplication of investments, absence of criteria to prioritize
investments, and only embryonic modal integration. This has led to bus services continuing to operate in
corrido,rs in which Flumitrens operates (especially since the introduction of the flat tariff in the
municipality), expensive and time-consuming disagreements on whether to build a light rail transport, the
lack of promotion for hub and spoke services, disparate subsidy policies often based on non-economic
considerations, and the absence of user representation in the urban transport decision-making process.
Recently, there has been an effort to integrate rail and intermunicipal bus services. The creation of a
Regionial Transportation Coordination Commission (RTCC) would ensure that this cooperation would not
depend on the political parties in power.
1.14         The lack of a coordinating entity with a unified approach to cost-recovery policies and
the lack of effective oversight by the State and the Municipality have resulted in the poor definition of
priorities in the RJMR, limited modal and tariff integration, and operating agencies which have no pre-set
operational and financial targets. The SRJ as part of this project has established the Ag6ncia
Metropolitana de Transporte Urbano (AMTU-RJ) which will fulfill the functions of a RTCC as described in
Annex 3. As required by the proposed project, the SRJ and RJM signed a convenio to create the AMTU-
RJ. A formal AMTU-RJ was established by a governor's resolution in January 1997.
D. Key Sectoral Issues
1.15         There are a number of key issues which must be addressed to improve the supply of
urban transport services and to guarantee their orderly development and sustainability in the long term in
the RJMR. They are: (a) capacity and safety issues; (b) institutional issues; (c) regulatory issues; (d) cost
recovery and financial management issues; (e) poverty alleviation and subsidy targeting; (f)
environmental issues; (g) transport planning issues; and (h) private sector participation in investments
and operations.
1.16         Capacity and Safety-Related Issues. First and foremost is the need to increase the
supply of adequate cost-efficient peak-hour capacity in order to guarantee an acceptable level of service
to the average commuter under reasonable safety conditions. This can be done by: (a) rehabilitating and
restructuring Flumitrens; (b) building new mass transit systems (busways, LRTs) which prove to be the
best alternative from the technical, economic and, especially, financial standpoint; (c) improving traffic



management through appropriate traffic engineering equipment in order to better manage available road
space and reduce congestion; and (d) reducing traffic and transit accidents through a combination of
transit safety education campaigns, law enforcement, and appropriate traffic engineering. The proposed
project would mainly strengthen Flumitrens' carrying capacity.
1.17         Institutional Issues. These are: (a) the fine-tuning of relations between state and
municipal governments and a clear definition of their respective roles in the financing, planning and
operation of urban transport services in accordance with the 1988 Constitution; (b) the creation of an
entity which is formally empowered by the RJMR for planning, coordinating and setting priorities for new
investments and modal integration including tariff setting and cost sharing, in order to assure a minimum
of planning continuity; and (c) the appropriate modification of regulation, including the elimination of any
remaining regulatory barriers which might prevent free entry and/or competition in the market for
provision of transport services especially bus services. The proposed project was instrumental in
achieving the establishment of an RTCC.
1.18         Cost Recovery and Financial Management Issues. The need to address cost
recovery from a more commercial-oriented standpoint by: (a) setting tariffs which, when added to
subsidies, cover at least the long-run variable costs (defined as out-of-pocket costs plus depreciation of
equipment and cost of capital) of the service provided and by controlling fare evasion; (b) setting
appropriate peak and off-peak pricing; (c) improving the financial management of the systems by wide
ranging cost-cutting measures, staff rationalization policies, and by appointing more financial managers
to run the mass transit systems; and (d) revamping funding mechanisms in order to guarantee adequate
financing for the implementation of new mass transit systems and the sustainability of the existing
systems. All the above issues would be addressed by the proposed project, through the concession.
1.19         Adequate Targeting of Subsidies. Blanket subsidies paid to operators, such as the
ones paid to Flumitrens/Metr6/CTC/CONERJ, ought to be eliminated. However, urban households have
been devoting increasingly large proportions of their incomes to transport, and transport-related
expenditures have become a considerable burden on local government budgets. The appropriate
targeting of urban transport subsidies and their extension to the informal sector which is not presently
covered by mechanisms such as the "vale-transporte" is a major issue which must be addressed in the
short term. Appropriate mechanisms for financing such subsidies (tariff differentiation, contracted
budgetary transfers, etc.) and channeling them (tokens, multimodal passes, differential tariffs) need to be
designed. Tariff policies, subsidies and financing mechanisms were carefully studied during the
preparation of the proposed loan, and a law to establish formal financing mechanisms for urban transport
was submitted to the state legislature.
1.20         Environmental Issues. A reduction of the environmental impacts of urban congestion
and noise pollution in the urban area would be brought about through cleaner and quieter systems. The
assessment of the potential role of market-based incentives to address pollution and implement, to the
extent possible, the polluter pay principle (PPP) to minimize the fiscal burden implied by de facto
govemment subsidies to polluters; design the allocation of responsibilities across government levels for
the enforcement of the law and definition of tougher standards. The Rio Special Environmental Control
Fund (FEEMA) must be equipped to monitor vehicle emissions and noise. An extensive environmental
review of the RJMR has been recently completed by the Bank and its recommendations have been taken
into account in this proposed loan.
1.21         Planning Issues. There is a need to strengthen RJMR's transportation planning, traffic
data base, traffic management, and the economic and financial evaluation of new investments,
especially the proposed systems for the Niter6i-S.Gongalo link and the extension of the Metr6 to Barra
da Tijuca. This could be done quite easily if there is a formal coordinating agency for urban transport in
the metropolitan region. The proposed project would strengthen planning capacities and the data bases
of the RJMR.
1.22         Private Sector Participation. The concession in full or by group of activities or sale to
the private sector of each urban transport agency and its assets, namely Flumitrensl
Metr6/CTC/CONERJ, might be a way to ensure that the level of service provided is respected and the
infrastructure and equipment are properly maintained. In Flumitrens, the maintenance of the rehabilitated



-6 -
fleet must be taken very seriously to avoid what is now happening (fleet availability is down to 58
percent). Furthermore, it is through privatization that some urban systems were able to arrest the chronic
fare evasion experienced in state-owned organizations. Early indications suggest fare evasion is
probably as much as 30 percent in Flumitrens. The SRJ should consider an increase in the private sector
participation in investment and operations of its systems. The concession of Flumitrens, Metr6 and
CONERJ and the sale of CTC were agreed upon in the State Reform Loan and would be consolidated
under the proposed project.
1.23         In the future, urban transport policies in the RJMR should be geared toward the efficient
and equitable provision of urban transport services, in particular for low-income groups which have
traditionally received less attention. Policies should be consistent with broad transport sector objectives,
as well as with the objectives of the urban subsector. In accordance with the 1988 Constitution, the
policies should support the decentralization of urban transport services from the Federal Government to
the St,ates to facilitate modal and tariff integration and respond quickly to local needs. The policies
should simultaneously address the need for institutional coordination and an integrated urban transport,
land use, and environmental strategy. The general approach is to restore the incentives which exist when
privately-owned systems operate under competitive conclitions. Redesigned regulations and
concessionary agreements, and improved bidding processes will promote competition in the provision of
bus services. Rates should be gradually liberalized in the corridors where there is effective competition.
Urban and metropolitan train operations should be owned by tlhe state and/or local governments and
operated by private interests operating under concessionary arrangements. Appropriate tariffs and
financing mechanisms would enable the efficient operation and development of the systems. Local
govemments would continue to be responsible for traffic regulation and management but would improve
their capacity to: (a) manage road use; (b) improve traffic management and street maintenance; (c)
protect the environment; (d) monitor the effects of regulations and taxation; (e) promote integration of the
various transport systems; and (f) coordination with land-use development.
E. Lessons from Experience in the Sector
1.24         Initial Bank support for the urban transport sector in Brazil was provided through four
urban transport projects totaling US$540 million, of which the First, Second and Third Urban Transport
Projects (Loans 1563-BR, 1839-BR, 1965-BR) have been cornpleted. The Fourth Urban Transport
Project, which continued the work started in the Third project, was canceled because the government
abolished the federal agency in charge of urban transport (EBTU). Additional urban transport projects
financed by the Bank (Loans 3457-BR and 3633-BR for US$126 million and US$128 million respectively)
support the rehabilitation and decentralization of the CBTU from the Federal to the State governments in
SAo Paulo and Rio de Janeiro. Loan 3457-BR (Sao Paulo) became effective on February 3, 1993. Loan
3633-BR (Rio de Janeiro) was signed October 14, 1993 and became effective March 14, 1994. Loan
3633-BR financed a number of studies dealing with the evaluation of options to concession out the
Flumitrens and Metr6 and contributed to the preparation of the presently proposed project.
1.25         To build upon the experience of past urban transport projects for the design of the
proposed project, an analysis of lessons learned was undertaken (Annex 1). The review was based on
Project Completion Reports and Performance Audit Reports (PCR/PAR) in urban transport and urban
development worldwide. Recognition was made of trends and changes in project design since the
PCRs/PARs were completed. The main lessons identified and incorporated into the design of the
proposed project are:
(a)   Institutional Strengthening. The organizations dealing with urban transport at
the federal, state and municipal levels should be reorganized and strengthened.
Studies included in the institutional component must be carefully monitored and
translated into action plans which the Borrower would implement;
(b)    Lack of counterpart funding has greatly influenced the pace of project
implementation and in some cases has led to cancellation of components. An
effort must be made to ensure that adequate provision of counterpart funds are
included in the annual budgets of federal and state enterprises;



- 7-
(c)    Slow implementation has been a frequently occurring theme. The reasons
have included: lack of familiarity with Bank procedures, over-optimistic
scheduling at appraisal, lack of final engineering designs at appraisal, changes
in political commitment, and lack of counterpart funds. These problems would be
mitigated in the proposed project by such measures as: strengthening capacity
of operating agencies for financial management and application of Bank
procedures; and requiring final engineering design of the first years works, for
which technical assistance should be contracted if need be with retroactive
financing; and
(d)    Rail Decentralization. Experience in both ongoing Lns. 3457-BR and 3633-BR
suggest that the decentralization of systems from the federal to the state level
must be followed by complete restructuring of the newly-formed companies
preferably with concessioning of the operations and maintenance of the systems
to the private sector. So far, the Buenos Aires concessioning of suburban rail
services and the subway to the private sector has substantially increased the
quality of the service provided and greatly reduced subsidies. Otherwise, there is
little motivation for government-appointed managers to reduce working subsidies
and improve the level of service offered by the systems.
F. The Bank's Strategy for the RJMR
1.26         Subsector strategy. In view of the issues stated above, the Bank's strategy would be
divided into short, medium and long term. In the short term: (a) assisting the state and municipal
govemments in formulating and implementing an urban transport policy consistent with the above goals;
(b) strengthening the institutional framework and the decision-making process in the sector through
adequate coordination between the two levels of government and proper technical, economic and
especially, financial evaluation of projects and their prioritization by a regional coordination agency; (c)
supporting a program to rehabilitate and extend existing services such as metro lines and busways,
which can more readily increase their efficiency and peak-hour capacity; (d) assisting in the concession
of the state-owned urban transport systems to the private sector; (e) managing the demand for road use
through improved traffic management and road-user taxes in order to reduce existing congestion and the
demand for travel by private single-occupancy automobiles; (f) decreasing the number of fatalities and
accidents due to urban transport; (g) introducing better cost-recovery mechanisms, including adequate
cost-based peak-hour tariffs and fare evasion control in order to strengthen their financial base; and (h)
improving the targeting of subsidies to those who need them. In the medium term, the Bank's strategy
will focus on promoting a more efficient and financially sustainable market-oriented supply of public
transport services, through: (a) reforming regulations which will facilitate market entry, fare deregulation,
and public bidding in route allocation; (b) promoting service differentiation especially in the bus systems;
(c) defining eligibility requirements and strengthening funding mechanisms for urban transport to cover
operating deficits (based on pre-agreed targets in contract plans) and finance major investments; (d)
supporting the evaluation and, if justified, the introduction of new investments such as reserved busways
and light rail transport; (e) reducing the environmental impacts of urban transport by setting standards for
air quality, noise pollution, and visual intrusion along with indicating who would pay for them and how;
and (f) assisting in projects which improve the data base required for appropriate planning such as
origin/destination surveys and preparation of master plans which study land use and urban transport
alternatives. In the long term, the Bank's strategy will aim at reducing the travel demand and increasing
the effectiveness of transport facilities by integrating land use and transport planning based on significant
input or management by locally-affected parties.



-8 -
II. THE PROJECT
A. Project Backgrouncl
2.1          As mentioned in Section I, this project has its origins in the decision of the Govemment
of Brazil to transfer the Rio de Janeiro subdivision of the Brazilian Urban Train Company to the State
Government, and the willingness of the State of Rio de Janeiro to better integrate urban trains with the
state-owned subway, the ferry system, and the bus transport system. In addition, faced with huge
subsidies to urban transport agencies (estimated at 8-10 percent of State revenues), the State embarked
on a far-reaching staff rationalization and concession program aided by the Bank's recently approved
State Reform loan.
B. Rationale for Bank Involvement
2.2          The Bank's Country Assistance Strategy for Brazil, discussed by the Board on June 12,
1997, is to support policies and investments that will encourage economic growth and social
development in a context of macroeconomic stability. The emphasis is on efficient resource allocation,
increased efficiency in the public sector, the appropriate targeting and delivery of support systems to the
low-income population; and on improving the creditworthiness of the Brazilian states. The proposed
project is fully consistent with this assistance strategy since it is designed to: (a) promote reforms and
financial viability of public enterprises, including concessions or sale of state-owned urban transport
agencies to the private sector; (b) foster private sector participation in the development of new
infrastructure and its operation; (c) increase the efficiency of infrastructure investments; (d) contribute to
poverty alleviation; and (e) reduce Government subsidies through improved tariff policies and financial
management. The proposed project is also a follow-up to the efforts started with the Rio de Janeiro
Metropolitan Transport Decentralization Project (Ln. 3633-BR) which decentralized the federally-owned
CBTU to the State of Rio de Janeiro to allow for more effective miodal and tariff integration.
C. Project Objectives
2.3          The main project objectives are: (a) to improve the quality of urban transport services in
the RJIMR by enhancing the development of a fully integrated urban transport system; and (b) to
substantially improve the level of service provided by Flumitrens while reducing the operating subsidies
it receives from the State through the substantial participation of the private sector in its operations and
management.
2.4          Objective (a) would be achieved by the rehabilitation/construction of transfer stations
between the several modes, the introduction of multimodal tariffs, and the establishment of a Regional
Transport Coordination Commission in charge of coordinating and recommending common policies on
pricing, financing and project evaluation in the RJMR. Objective (b) would be achieved through an
investment program for the rehabilitation/modernization of Flumitrens facilities and equipment and a full
or partial concession of the operations and management of the railway to the private sector as a means
of reducing the subsidies Flumitrens receives from the State.
2.5          Subsidiary objectives would be the improvement of the mobility of the low-income
population who are the main user of Flumitrens and public transport, and the reduction of the
environrnental impacts (mainly air quality and noise) on the RJMR due to road-based vehicle use.
D. Project Description
2.6          The project would comprise two components:
(a)    an Infrastructure and Equipment component (96 percent of the total project
cost) including: (i) rehabilitating/refurbishing of Flumitrens' rolling stock and
infrastructure; and (ii) rehabilitating or building transfer stations between the
trains and other modes (Metro, buses and ferries); and



- 9 -
(b)    an Institutional and Policy Development component (4 percent of the total
project cost) to help in: (i) supporting the process of concessioning Flumitrens to
the private sector through technical assistance for the preparation and
evaluation of bids; (ii) operating the RTCC for the RJMR; (iii) preparing an
integrated Transport Policy, Land Use and Air Quality Management strategy for
the RJMR to meet both transport and air quality targets and to introduce sound
cost-recovery, tariff, regulatory and subsidy policies; (iv) a comprehensive
retraining program for Flumitrens staff in view of the planned restructuring of the
company; (v) preparing feasibility studies and bidding documents for the
proposed Niter6i-S.Gon,alo extension and other priorities of the mass transit
system; and (vi) implementing an Environmental and Traffic subcomponent
including: (a) a study to review the ongoing vehicle inspection/maintenance
program; and (b) a comprehensive review of traffic violations with emphasis on
a more reliable system to collect traffic violation fines and enforce them as a
deterrent to traffic accidents.
Part A - Infrastructure and Equipment Component
2.7           This component represents 96 percent of the total project cost and is designed to
improve the infrastructure and equipment of Flumitrens to a level which is deemed necessary to attract
potential private sector concessionaires as summarized below.
2.8           The Civil Works Program which would account for 8 percent of Part A component
would consist of: (a) rehabilitation and modernization of 11 stations; (b) construction of two stations; (c)
construction of five pedestrian over/underpasses; and (d) eight km of fences in areas adjacent to stations
to reduce evasion.
2.9           The Permanent Way Program which would account for 2 percent of Part A includes
installation of sleepers, and the superstructure for the second track in Gramacho-Saracuruna.
2.10         The Systems Program which would account for 21 percent of Part A would consist of:
(a) installation and repair of transmission lines in the Leopoldina corridor; (b) rehabilitation/construction of
electric substations; (c) installation of Central Traffic Control (CTC) in D. Pedro II and Deodoro yards and
Sta. Cruz and Japeri corridors; (d) the installation of Automatic Train Control system in Deodoro, Sta.
Cruz and Japeri corridors; (e) automatic fare collection; and (f) telecommunications, with the installation
of fiber optics cable links in the Japeri corridor, the provision of a central station, and an integrated
telecommunication system in the CCO.
2.11         The Rolling Stock Program, which would account for 65 percent of Part A, would
consist of the rehabilitation of 103 Electrical Multiple Units (EMUs), the refurbishment of 36 EMUs.
Part B - Institutional Development and Policy
2.12         This component is designed to assist the State in the continuation of the implementation
of the concessions and management contracts jump-started by the proposed State Reform project for
Flumitrens, Metr6, CONERJ and CTC lines or services. A package of institutional and policy reforms is
needed to induce modernization changes and motivate management and staff. In addition this
component would be used to strengthen the regional transport coordination commission to ensure that
modal integration, tariff and subsidy policies, and prioritization of future urban transport projects in the
region become a reality. This component would account for 4 percent of the total project cost.



- 10 -
2.13         Flumitrens-Related Studies.
(a)    Assisting the Concession to the Private Sector. The first set of Actions Plans
concems the preparation of bid documents to be used in the concession
process: (i) institutional and organizational arrangements; (ii) investment plan to
be undertaken; (iii) evaluation of operating subsidies; (iv) patrimonial agreement
on the valuation and assets to transferred; (v) aspects relating to transfer of
personnel, pension benefits, and miscellaneous human resource-related issues;
and (vi) legal and administrative aspects for concession;
(b)    Technical Assistance Until the Concession. This technical assistance would
provide key advisors who would work with the President of Flumitrens to further
rationalize and stream line operations and maintenance to decrease the
perceived risks of the concession. For example, hiring of experts in rolling stock
maintenance and railways operations mianagement, management accounting to
define clear cost allocation rules, specialists to implement the payroll software,
and experts to build a reliable data base;
(c)    Technical Engineering Studies in Signaling and Telecom. These studies
would address the needs identified during preparation for the installation of
existing and new signaling and telecom equipment; and
(d)    Supervision Consultants. The implementation of the investment plan would be
supervised by engineering consultants in charge of ensuring that the works and
goods comply with specifications; and Project Management Consultants.
These consultants would be in charge of supporting the Project Management
and Implementation Group until the completion of the Project.
2.14         Modal Integration Related Study. The objective of the second set of Action Plans is to
support the establishment of the RTCC policy framework and provide practical recommendations for
implementing modal integration, namely: (a) updating of the Transport, Land Use, and Air Quality portion
of the Master Plans of the RJMR with emphasis on route rationalization and modal and tariff integration;
(b) an intermunicipal route management and rationalization study; and (c) preparation of feasibility
studies and bidding documents for the Niter6i - Sao Gongalo rnass transportation system. The RJMR
master plan should be analyzed in order to give the basic guidelines for the development of future
systeims for this corridor.
2.15         Training. In view of the concession, staff who remain with Flumitrens holding would
have two primary functions: (a) to manage the implementation of the investment plan; and (b) to monitor
the concessionaire on behalf of the State Regulatory Entity (ASEP-RJ).
2.16         Environmental and Traffic Safety Program. The environmental and traffic safety
prognam is estimated to cost US$1.1 million and accounts for 0.4 percent of the total project cost. It
would consist of: (a) a study to review the ongoing inspection and maintenance (I/M) program for vehicle
emissions and noise and propose corrective measures; and (b) a comprehensive review of traffic
violations with emphasis on a more reliable system to collect traffic violation fines and enforce them as a
deterrent to traffic accidents.
E. Project Cost and Financing
2.17         The total cost of the project is estimated at about US$373 million including physical and
price contingencies, with an estimated foreign component of US$186 million or about 50 percent of total
cost. Taxes and duties are expected to account for about 15 percent of total costs or US$57 million
equivalent. Physical contingencies add up to US$30 million, or 9 percent of the base cost, and price
contingencies are estimated at US$19 million or 6 percent of the base cost plus physical contingencies.
The contingencies were estimated on the basis of the disbursement schedule (an estimated
implernentation period of six years) and on the following forecast of price escalation for both local and
foreign expenditures expressed in US dollars: 2.9 percent in 1996, 2.0 percent in 1997, 2.1 percent in
1998, 2.3 percent in 1999, 2.4 percent in 1999, 2.4 percent in 2000 and 2.3 percent in 2001. Base costs
correspond to January 1996 prices. The costs of goods are based upon supplier quotations for similar



- 1 1 -
Brazilian-made items and recent prices of imported items. The cost of several components of the works
program such as, permanent way, two new stations, and the administrative building are based upon
detailed engineer designs and prevailing unit prices; the cost of other components of the works program
are based upon preliminary engineering designs and prevailing unit prices from recent tenders under
Loans 3457-BR and 3633-BR. Final engineering designs for the remaining components under the project
are underway. Cost estimates for consultant services are based upon prevailing local and foreign staff-
months rate; they provide for the cost of local transportation, office equipment, other minor items, and
where appropriate, intemational travel and per diem.

2.1El        The project would be financed from the propiosed Bank loan of US$186 million (50
percent of the project cost) and the SRJ contributions of about US$187 million equivalent, including
US$57 million in taxes and duties (50 percent of the project cost). During loan negotiations, agreement
was reached on: (a) the project financing plan; and (b) the guarantee of the SRJ regarding all necessary
counterpart funds for the project.
F. Project Benefits and Risks
2.19         Project Benefits. Quantifiable and non-quantifiable benefits are expected from the
provision of an integrated rail and bus network including cost savings, time savings for the users, fewer
accidents and fatalities, and employment generation through better spatial integration of markets.
Important effects on the urban structure are expected if the RJMR rail based network becomes an axis
fed by the major bus routes. In addition, there would be a positive environmental impact through the
reduction of fossil fuel consumption, reduction of motor-vehicle related emissions, and a reduction in
road congestion, with a consequent improvement in air quality and noise pollution. The project would
facilitate access to employment and services, particularly for the low-income population. Development of
an integrated public transport system under a regional coordination body in charge of combining state
and municipal pricing and subsidy policies, prioritizing major investments, and proposing common
demand management measures in the RJMR, are important non-quantifiable benefits. Finally, by
concessioning out to the private sector the operations and maintenance of Flumitrens, the proposed
project would be a first step towards decreasing the investment and subsidy burden on the state.
2.20         Project Risks. The usual risks associated with investment projects-cost and time
overruns-apply to this project. The investment, however, is in well-known technology, available from
many sources. Delays in the procurement process were minimized by starting at pre-appraisal the pre-
qualification for lots in the critical path of project implementation, by the use of standard bidding
documents, and by requiring the borrower to produce the major tender documents by negotiations.
Further risks include problems with the formal establishment and sustainability of the RTCC, possible
delays in the procurement process and in the rehabilitation of Flumitrens' rolling stock (which has a direct
impact on demand), delays in the actual award or failure to concession out Flumitrens, delays in
controlling fare evasion, and the timely availability of counterpart funds. Negotiations between the State
and Municipal Governments have progressed very well for modal and tariff coordination and a
forerunner of the RTCC has already been established. All parties have agreed on the need for an
integrated urban transport system but there is still a risk that the involved parties will be reluctant to rely
on R1CC's consensus and advice due to political differences between the State Government and the
Mayor of Rio d Janeiro. It is, however, expected that just the discussion of the main urban transport
issues and recommendations of the RTCC will generate pressure for politicians to seek metropolian
rather than state-only or municipal-only solutions. The pre-qualification documents for rolling stock
rehabilitation were already issued, and several trains rehabilitated under Ln. 3633-BR will be available
before! the concession. The contracting of the concession of Flumitrens is a condition for the
disbursement of the second tranche of the State Reform Project. The timely availability of counterpart
funds would be addressed by seeking assurances that they are included in the SRJ's CY97 and CY98
budgets and future budget projections. If the concession process fails, the repercussions are: (a) the
State-appointed management of Flumitrens may not have the independence required to run the railway
as a commercially-oriented concern. This might affect the service and the control of fare evasion having,
therefore, an impact on net revenues; and (b) part of the proposed investment would have been
committed. The proposed investment would always be beneficial to the railway because it would be
mainly in rolling stock. In case the award of the concession does not materialize, the Bank would press
for the second best altemative which calls for substantial outsourcing of railway maintenance activities. If
this is the case, the economic rate of return of the project would still be high (33 percent). In case the
concession fails to materialize, the fall in ridership would bring the economic rate of return down to 15
percent.
G. Tariff Setting and Affordability by Low-income Users
2.21         Poverty, Affordability and Subsidies. The growing crisis in urban transportation in
Brazil, especially in the metropolitan regions, demands a close look at alternatives for financing services
in a more predictable and sustainable way while, at the same time, targeting subsidies more directly to
low-inoDme ridership. The various initiatives already underway within ongoing Lns. 3457-BR and 3633-



- 13 -
BR to improve efficiency through tariff increases, service improvements, and trip reliability, as well as
efforts focused on reducing operating costs, are planned to continue.
2.22         Approximately 56 percent of the urban population earns less than four minimum salaries
(US$400/month). If successfully implemented, the project would primarily benefit the low-income
population in the participating cities, since they depend mainly on bus, metro and suburban rail services
to commute to and from work, especially those living in the favelas for whom access to the areas of
employment is a daily ordeal.
2.23         To make urban transport affordable to the low-income population, it is necessary to
strengthen and expand the eligibility of the vale-transporte program to low-income residents which are
not formally employed, to substantially promote integrated (bus-rail) fares, which should be always lower
than the sum of individual modal fares, and to increase the overall level of service provided to the users.
This would result in lower travel times due to higher frequencies and operating speeds, reduction of
waiting time, much higher reliability and safety, bringing down therefore, the overall generalized (tariff,
time and safety) transport cost to the user and thus alleviating the burden that urban transportation
imposes on low-income households. It is noteworthy that annual bus and train passenger surveys
indicate even low-income riders are willing to pay higher tariffs if the service provided is faster, waiting
times are less, and the system is more reliable.
2.24         In the bidding documents for the concession of Flumitrens, the govemment would set the
tariff to be charged by the concessionaire to guarantee that it is affordable by the predominately low-
income population populations which constitute the main ridership of the Flumitrens system. The tariff
would be updated regularly to reflect inflation and would be pegged to the urban bus tariff which is
periodically updated. The concession contracts will oblige the private concessionaires of the bus, rail,
metro and ferries to negotiate joint tariffs which are less than the sum of individual tariffs.
2.25         Under the State Reform Project, the State has commissioned a study to recommend the
most appropriate tariff policies for the RJMR in a context where there would be several privates
concessionaires. The study will propose ways of introducing a multi-modal ticket and how to allocate its
proceeds amongst the several concessionaires.
2.26         Review of Financing Mechanisms. In conclusion, it was agreed that efforts should be
focused on seeking a solution(s) that would: (a) generate sufficient resources to maintain stable and
predictable funding for urban transport investments; and (b) establish mechanisms to generate adequate
operating cross-subsidies from within transport related taxes, user charges, etc., to provide adequate
support for normalization (operating deficits) transfers. The SRJ has submitted to its legislature a draft
law proposing financing mechanisms for funding urban transport operating subsidies while they exist and
eventual future expansions.
H. Project Economic Evaluation
2.27         A standard cost-benefit analysis was performed to evaluate the economic impact of the
implementation of the proposed Investment and Equipment program, which together represent 96
percent of the total proposed project. Direct user benefits include travel time and operating costs
savings; indirect benefits arise from the reduction of negative externalities such as accident and air
pollution costs. Investment costs comprise civil works, permanent way, operational systems, rolling stock
and project studies.
2.28         Substantial direct benefits are expected to arise from travel time savings, operating cost
savings, bus system control savings, and road maintenance cost savings. Travel time savings result
mainly from the transfer of bus passengers into the railway system and from existing Flumitrens
passengers who can save time in their trips by taking advantage of the improved transport facility. Travel
time savings also result from the increased operating speeds and shorter trips of the buses and cars
which would be feeding the rail system. Operating cost savings for the non-rail modes derive principally
from the improved commercial and traffic speeds, which can be achieved by buses and cars,
respectively. In addition, indirect benefits would arise from the reduction in negative externalities, such
as accidents and air pollution.

I. Project Financial Evaluation
2.30         Flumitrens. CBTU/STU-RJ, the predecessor of Flumitrens, historically relied on the
Federal Government to finance its operational deficits and its capital program. These operational deficits
have been attributed to low and declining ridership, insufficient tariffs, high personnel costs, and
significant rates of evasion and fraud. During 1991-95 the cost recovery from internally generated funds
varied between 11-19 percent. Personnel expenses accounted for 84 percent of the total working costs',
and the total revenues were barely enough to cover one-fourth of the working costs. Actual ridership in
1995 was about 370,000 paying passengers per day compared to the nearly one million paying
passengers in 1984. Evasion and ticket fraud was estimated at 17-22 percent in 1995 and contributed to
the poor working ratio of 6.12. Flumitrens received US$186 million in operating subsidies in 1995.
2.31         The present management of Flumitrens has put in place a new cost reduction program to
rationalize the work-force and to cut expenditures. Between 1995-1996, about 2,000 staff were let go
through various incentive programs. Similarly, overtime compensation was reduced by 10 percent in
1996. In order to better control ticket evasion and fraud, Flumitrens is in the process of outsourcing ticket
sales in its major stations. Administrative functions are ailso being re-evaluated for possible
rationalization. On the revenue side, an extensive capital investment program is under way for
increasing the capacity of the system through the recovery of paralyzed trains. In addition, Flumitrens is
actively pursuing integration among different modes of transport (bus+train, train+metro) to take
advantage of its strength as a low-cost producer. A private concessionaire is expected to be operating
Flumitrens as of January 1, 1998.
2.32         Upon completion of the proposed Bank project and other ongoing investment programs,
the railway is not only expected to recapture all of its lost traffic but also attract some new ridership. The
paying ridership is expected to increase by about 250 percent between 1996-2002 through a combination
of better management, increased capacity, and reduction of ticket evasion. Additional revenues would be
provided by an increase of the standard tariff to US$0.55, salie of advertisement space, and rental
income from the stations. The availability of extra capacity, however, is not expected to result in an
automatic increase in the ridership; the private concessionaire would have to change the image of the
suburban rail by offering timely and reliable service. It is expected that by the fourth year of the
concession, the railway would be in a position to operate without any operating subsidies from the State
Governrnent. Performance targets to monitor Flumitrens' financial situation during the project are given
below.

2.33          State of Rio de Janeiro Finances. The finances of the State of Rio de Janeiro were
studied in detail in the context of the Rio de Janeiro State Reform Project and the analysis shows that
the State has improved its financial situation and would be able to provide the necessary counterpart
funds for the proposed loan as well the eventual subsidies which might be needed during the project time
horizon. Furthermore, the State would have the capability of, prior to concession, providing Flumitrens
with a budget which would allow the execution of routine and periodic maintenance required to improve
train availability. Annex I OB gives details of the State financial analysis.
J. Environmental Impact of the Project
2.34          The proposed project is classified as environmental category B. Since the proposed
project would greatly increase the capacity offered by the RJMR rail system, it is expected that a
substantial percentage of the passengers now carried by bus will return to their original mode of
transportation, that is, rail. This is especially true for passengers who live within walking distance from
the stations, who switched to buses because in the last five years the capacity and reliability offered by
the trains decreased considerably. Nevertheless, theoretically it is estimated that by project completion
about 1,789 fewer buses/day would be required in the streets of the RJMR, because of shifting demand
and because bus integration with the Flumitrens system would shorten bus trips. Intermunicipal buses
would operate feeder services for the rail system rather than duplicate its routes. The main expected
positive environmental impacts stem from this reduction in bus services, given their present negative
impact on the air quality, noise levels, traffic congestion, and road accidents. Population resettlement is
not foreseen given that works are in existing right-of-ways which have not been invaded. Independent
consultants physically inspected of the right-of-way and confirmed that no resettlement is needed. The
concession of Flumitrens would be preceded by an environmental and safety audit to be carried out by
independent consultants and following terms of reference provided by the Bank.
2.35          The impact on air quality is due to a reduction of emission by the buses which would be
removed from circulation or which would have their route kms shortened because they would be
operating feeder services for the rail system rather than duplicating their routes. The impact on air quality
was conservatively estimated at US$1.2 million per year in RJMR. A positive impact on noise levels is
also due to the removal of buses, especially in RJM, where the older vehicles predominantly owned by
the private sector are noisy and when bunched together cause decibel (dB) levels, which preclude
speaking at normal voice levels on the street. The impact on traffic congestion was estimated at
US$91.6 million per year in RJM and is mainly due to the shorter waiting time for commuters using their
automobiles and buses. The effect on road accidents is based on statistics which suggest that in the
metropolitan region, the number of bus accidents is a function of the number of buses on the road. It is
conservatively estimated that the savings due to fewer accidents are US$2.2 million per year in the RJM.
Even if the benefits estimated were cut in half because the bus operators would, in some cases, continue
to run the buses at lower load factors, the project would contribute positively to the environment. Benefits
which were not quantified are those related to the improved living environment which would result from
safer and cleaner stations, crossovers from the sidewalks to the stations, space for queuing up at the
wickets, ramps for the handicapped, and in some cases, modal integration with taxis, buses and private
automobiles. Other non-quantified benefits are those related to the structuring effect of a revamped rail



- 16 -
system which would attract housing in the surrounding areas. Furthermore, real estate development on
the air space above the stations and on land owned by. Flumitrens is also expected. Additional non-
quantified benefits relate to the diminished stress which the road system and congestion would impart on
the road users and the better ambiance expected in trains. Shorter trip times and faster home-to-work
trips would also have a positive effect on the rail users' welfare.
K. Private Sector Participaltion
2.36         As part of the proposed State Reform Project, the SRJ has decided to concession out to
the private sector Flumitrens and the Metr6 and to sell its assets in CONERJ (ferries) and CTC/SERVE
(buses), and concession the routes they operated to the private sector. To make Flumitrens and the
Metr8 more attractive to the private sector, the SRJ started a number of reforms. The reforms would
focussed essentially on Flumitrens and the Metr6 which together are the main contributors to 80 percent
of the operating deficit of the sector. The overall strategy to be followed in both cases is the following:
(a)    staff downsizing through voluntary and involuntary dismissals;
(b)   reduction of salary benefits to the minimum required by law;
(c)    acceleration of private sector involvement in operations and management of the
urban transport companies, through concessions and/or management contracts;
(d)   tariff adjustments to increase revenues in line with inflation; and
(e)    sale/lease land assets and building which belong to the agencies to increase
non-operating revenues.
2.37         Flumitrens and Metr6. As a top priority, the SRJ has set January 1998 as the deadline
for starting the long-term concession of the Metr8 and July 1998 for the long-term concession of
Flumitrens. To make both systems more aftractive to the private sector, the State requested that both
Flumitrens and the Metr6 put in place a downsizing program since the ratio 'revenue passengers per
staff' is low when compared to similar systems elsewhere, suggesting that payroll costs should be
reduced by almost 50 percent or the revenue passengers increased by as much. The initial staff
reduction program was completed in May 1996 with a reduction of 1800 staff in Flumitrens and 800 in
Metr8. 1-he State has used as a model and comparator for the Metr6, the Santiago, Chile subway which
has approximately the same length, almost twice as many passengers at the same average tariff and
has an operating surplus. For Flumitrens the model chosen as a comparator is the Buenos Aires
Suburbain rail system which is now operated by private concessionaires and has a productivity three
times higlher than Flumitrens. In addition, the SRJ asked Flumitrens to put in place a fare evasion control
system to increase revenues. The State has also instructed the agencies to evaluate the land and
buildings which can be sold or leased to increase non-operating revenues.
2.38         To decide the type of private management that would be more adequate for both
companies, the SRJ has launched two studies to evaluate the options for private sector participation in
the companies and to prepare the bidding documents for the selected option. These studies were
financed with funds from the ongoing Bank Loan (Ln. 3633-BR), which financed the decentralization of
the rail system from Federal to State Governments. The SRJ feels that the present State-appointed
administrations of Flumitrens and Metr6 are constrained by existing legislation from undertaking bolder
efforts to restructure their companies. The SRJ believes that a long term concession of both Flumitrens
and the Metr6 would reduce annual subsidies and improve quality of service as it did in Buenos Aires. To
make both systems more attractive to potential private sector concessionaires, the SRJ has instructed
Flumitrens and Metro's management to implement the above-mentioned downsizing programs which by
May 1996 had exceeded the targets set by the Government.
2.39         Concessions of the Flumitrens and Metro would likely follow the Argentinean model
which calls for bids to execute an investment plan financed and defined by the State, propose a schedule
of decreasing operating subsidies to be paid by the State, and provide a level-of-service stipulated in the
bidding documents at a tariff set by the Government. The proponent with the lowest net present value of
the cost of investment plan plus the subsidy is the winner of the concession. The Argentinean model



-17-
calls for a schedule of subsidies which become zero after a certain date, at which time the
concessionaire must start paying the govemment an annual concession fee. Suburban railway
concessions in Argentina were granted for 10 years and the subway concession was granted for 20
years. SRJ has hired a financial advisor to carry out the concession process of both Flumitrens and the
Metro and the process is underway. Consultants hired by the State are now preparing the model for the
concession and the bidding documents, following Bank's procurement guidelines.
2.40         The main difference between Flumitrens and Metro is that the latter has already secured
the funds for completing the works required to double its ridership and increase its route length from 23
to 35 km, while the former would be receiving those funds under the proposed Rio de Janeiro Mass
Transit Project. The investment for the Metro (US$298 million) came from a BNDES loan (the National
Economic and Social Development Bank), and would be used primarily for completing works started
more than 10 years ago which were halted due to the financial situation of the State. The investment for
Flumitrens (US$373 million) would be mainly devoted to rehabilitate rolling stock and upgrading the
signaling, telecom and stations necessary for the system to reach a ridership of one million passengers
per day.
2.41         CONERJ. The ferry boat company would have its assets sold to the highest bidder.
SECTRAN would act as the overseer of the private sector concessionaires which would operate the ferry
lines in the Guanabara and Sepetiba bays. The SRJ has set a date of November 1997 for completing
this sale, and has commissioned a valuation study using its own funds.
2.42         CTC and SERVE have been liquidated. Their assets are mainly buses and garages
which were sold to the private sector. The routes operated by CTC and SERVE would be taken over by
existing bus concessionaires which would be selected through competitive bidding.
2.43         CODERTE, the agency in charge of the terminals and parking garages of the State,
would also be sold. The SRJ has decided to undertake a study to determine the value of the assets
involved, to obtain all the legal titles of the properties, and to prepare terms of reference to select a real
estate company which would sell or rent the properties owned by the SRJ.
2.44         State Regulatory Entity (ASEP-RJ). The state has decided in the context of the State
Reform Project to create a State Regulatory Entity (ASEP-RE) in charge of regulation of all industries
including urban transport. This entity would in the beginning delegate some of its monitoring functions to
the Secretariat of Transport but will retain the main regulatory functions (entry, exit, disputes, and tariff
regulation). The AMTU-RJ would provide input to the ASEP-RJ whenever needed.



-18 -
Ill. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. Institutional Responsibiliities
3.1          The State of Rio de Janeiro would be the Borrower. The Federative Republic of Brazil
would be the Guarantor. Flumitrens or its successor would be responsible for implementing the project, in
accordance with: (a) contractual arrangements; (b) the implemientation schedule; (c) implementation
arrangements; (d) the decentralization process; (e) procurement arrangements; (f) disbursement
arrangements; (g) auditing arrangements; and (h) monitoring and reporting arrangements. These
arrangements were confirmed during negotiations, and detailed guidelines, satisfactory to the Bank, were
prepared by Flumitrens. The Bank would monitor the implementation of the project in accordance with a
monitoring and supervision plan (Annex 11).
3.2          The overall coordination and supervision of the proposed project would rest with
Flumitrens which has established a Project Management and Implementation Group (PMIG) supported
by consultants. Flumitrens and the State agreed that the PMIG would continue to manage the execution
of the project after the concession of the Flumitrens system to the private sector. This would ensure the
continuity of project management experience acquired with the ongoing Ln. 3633-BR and the State
Reform Projects and would prevent delays which could occur due a change of the project
implementation team before the conclusion of the project. To nnanage and guide the "non-hardware"
aspects (political, legal, administrative, institutional, and so forth) through the transition and until
December 1997, an intragovernmental General Coordination Committee would coordinate the work of a
number of task forces, which would carry out strategic studies on terms of reference agreed with the
Bank and prepare the bidding documents for the concessions.
B. Contractual Arrangements
3.3          In order to ensure a smooth transition to concession, and to support its subsequent
consolidation, the following legal arrangements would apply:
With the Bank:
Bank and GOB                            (Guarantor)          Guarantee Agreement
13ank and SRJ                           (Borrower)           Loan Agreement
Between governments and agencies:
SRJ and Flumitrens                      (Subsidiary Agreement and Contract Program)
Flumitrens and Private Concessionaire   (Concession Contract)
C. Implementation Schedule
3.4          The implementation of this project has been tentatively scheduled over a five-year
period (1997-2001). The project would start on November 1, 1997' and be completed by December 31,
2001, wilth a closing date of June 30, 2002. The Bank has recommended that Flumitrens should take
special care in finalizing the project implementation schedule, which is directly linked to the procurement
process, and indicated to Flumitrens that according to the Bank's experience, the bidding process for
each bid may take from 7 to 12 months, including the approval of bidding documents, advertisement in
Developrnent Business and local press, bid evaluation, recommendations for award, contract signature,
and contract effectiveness.
D. Implementation Arrangements
3.5          Project Management and Implementation Group (PMIG). Flumitrens would establish
and maintain a Project Management and Implementation Group (PMIG) headed by a Project Coordinator
to manage the implementation of the several components of the Bank project. The Project Coordinator
would relport directly to Flumitrens' President. Project Managemient and Implementation consultants
financed under Part A of the project would act as full-time advisers to the Project Coordinator to provide
the technical support and cross-country experience required for managing the project. These consultants
should be hired immediately after pre-appraisal. In addition to the Project Coordinator who would be
responsible for the overall financial management of the project, the PMIG would include: (a) a transport



- 19 -
economist and policy studies coordinator responsible for overseeing the execution of all the studies and
transport economics aspects of Part A of the project; (b) an engineer, with experience in procurement, to
coordinate Part B of the project; and (c) a management information systems specialist responsible for
the reporting and supervision of the project. The PMIG would be supported by a small secretariat which
would include an executive secretary and an accountant responsible for project accounts and
disbursements.
E. Concession Process
3.6          Until the concessionaire takes over, Flumitrens would continue to be managed by the
present SRJ-appointed management which would be responsible to implement the reforms described
above. Flumitrens and the SRJ would sign a contract program in which Flumitrens management would
agree to meet targets set by the SRJ. After the concession to the private sector, a Flumitrens Entity in
charge of supervising the concession and implementing the investment plan financed by the proposed
loan would remain in place and SRJ will decide, until December 1997, whether it will be part of ASEP-RJ
or/and SECTRAN. The PMIG would be part of this Flumitrens Entity together with a group of supervisors
in charge of monitoring the concessionaire. The provisional organizational structure and composition of
the Flumitrens Entity is presented in Annex 2 and will be confirmed at negotiations.
3.7          Transitional Phase to Concession. As the successful concession would be subject to
detailed negotiations, and would involve political considerations in addition to financial and technical
issues, the detailed steps and transition to concession are viewed as a dynamic process involving the full
and transparent participation of the Secretaries of Planning, Transport and Flumitrens. In order to
support this process, joint and parallel strategic studies, administrative and legal actions, organizational
changes, and key political decisions will have to be undertaken in a logical and sequential manner, and
synchronized with the timetable for processing and executing the proposed Bank loan to ensure the
completion of the concession process of Flumitrens not later than the target date of July 31, 1998. The
following steps should be included:
(a) General Coordination and Completion of Strategic Studies. The General Coordination
Committee (CG) would manage and guide the concession process. The CG would incorporate a number
of task forces. The CG, with representatives from Flumitrens, SECPLAN and SECTRAN, would report to
the SRJ Governor. The task forces would work on the strategic studies and Actions Plans necessary to
prepare the bidding process.
2.45  (b)    Concession to Private Sector. In accordance with the SRJ Government policy for
modemization of the economy, Flumitrens would be concessioned out using procurement rules
acceptable to the Bank. Assurances were sought at negotiations that not later than 60 days after signing
of the loan, the SRJ would issue pre-qualification bids for the concession, and thereafter, not later than
July 31, 1998 the concession would be signed. The concession of Flumitrens would be preceded by an
environmental and safety audit to be carried out by independent consultants and following terms of
reference provided by the Bank.
3.1          Timetable for the Concession process. The project has been prepared to take into
account the SRJ Government's objective to complete concession of the Flumitrens by the target date of
July 31, 1998 . Until the concession is completed the SRJ will be allowed to invest up to US$50 million or
27% of the loan on the infrastructure and equipment component, mainly on rolling stock. The term
"concession" means the transfer of operational and maintenance responsibilities of the Flumitrens
System to a Consortium including the enactment and execution of all legal, contractual and
administrative acts and documents required under the legislation of the State and of the Guarantor for
these purposes. If the first attempt at concessioning out the system fails for reasons beyond the control
of SRJ, the Borrower will attempt again to concession out the system and have a concession signed not
later than December 31, 1999. In the meantime , the Bank may authorize SRJ to invest up to 50% of the
loan, mainly on rolling stock, as long as SRJ/Flumitrens outsource the maintenance of rolling stock and
the fare collection to the private sector and comply with the targets for availability of rolling stock,
interval between trains and the operating subsidy provided in Table 2.3. If by December 31, 1999, no
concession has been signed and the targets agreed were not met, the Bank may cancel the remainder of
the loan.

3.3           Procurement procedures would be as follows:
(a)    contracts for goods estimated to cost more than US$350,000 equivalent, and
contracts for works including civil works, systems, permanent way and rolling
stock, estimated to cost more than US$5.0 million equivalent, would be procured
through International Competitive Bidding (ICB) procedures in accordance with
Bank guidelines. The procurement of some goods valued below US$350,000
could be through National Competitive Bidding (NCB) procedures up to an
aggregate amount of US$0.5 million;
(b)    works of US$5.0 million equivalent or Iless, up to an aggregate amount of
US$37.1 million (Table 3.1) would be aEwarded through NCB procedures. In
addition: (i) project components such as, civil works and systems, permanent
way, and rolling stock have been packaged and would be procured under supply
and installation procedures; and (ii) pre-qualification procedures would be
followed for the procurement of the signaling component; and
