-47-                                  ANNEX4
9.           CONERJ operates the ferryboat system  between Rio/Niter6i, Rio/Paqueta and
Rio/Ribeira in the Guanabara Bay and the route between Mangaratiba/Abrago/Angra dos Reis in the
Sepetiba Bay. CONERJ also administers and operates the ferry terminals of Praga XV in Rio, Niter6i,
Paqueta, Ribeira, and also the docking quays in Mangaratiba, Abraao and Angra dos Reis. CONERJ has
19 ferries of which eleven have a 2,000 passenger capacity, four have a capacity of 1,000, two of 500,
two of 370 and 3 support vessels. In 1995, CONERJ had 1,097 employees of which 381 were in
administrative functions and 716 in operating functions. Most of the passengers (93 percent) are carried
in the Rio (Praga XV) -Niter6i link which at peak hours offers a 15 minute headway service for a 5 km (20
minute) crossing. CONERJ transported roughly 24 million passengers per year and in 1995 had a
subsidy of R$29.64 (US$30.875) which gives a subsidy per passenger of R$1.2 (US$1.286) per
passenger. SRJ intends to sell the assets of CONERJ and concession out its present lines to the private
sector.
10.          CTCISERVE are bus companies which operate in the municipalities of Rio de Janeiro,
Niter6i, Sao Gongalo and Campos providing intermunicipal services and integrated services with other
modes. CTC also operates in the Santa Teresa neighborhood of Rio where it operates the tramways
(bondinho). CTC/SERVE operate a total of 26 bus routes and 2 tramway routes. They have a fleet of 369
buses of which 117 are fueled by gas, 50 are articulated diesel-fueled buses and 142 are standard diesel
buses with more than 7 years. In 1995, CTC/SERVE had a total of 2,496 employees of which 1,048 in
administrative functions and 1448 in operating functions. CTC/SERVE staffing per bus is much higher
than that of its private sector competitors who do not receive subsidies. CTC/SERVE transported 26.6
million passengers in 1995 and received subsidies which amounted to R$36.79 (US$38.32) or a subsidy
of US$1.441 per passenger carried. The State has decided to sell CTC/SERVE assets and concession
out its lines to the private sector operators.
11.          CODERTE owns 18 road terminals, 2 garage buildings in Rio, 12 parking lots located in
several municipalities, in addition to shops, rooms and commercial real estate totaling 200 properties.
The road terminals of Novo Rio and Roberto da Silveira are privately managed. At the end of 1995,
CODERTE had 695 employees of which 306 in administrative functions and 389 in operating functions.
In 1995, CODERTE turned an operating profit of R$1.21 million (US$1.26 million). The State feels that
CODERTE could be much more profitable and therefore it plans to increase private sector participation
in the management of CODERTE's properties and to sell some of the assets to the private sector.
12.          DETRO is the agency in charge of regulation of the intermunicipal bus system in the
State. There are two types of services regulated by DETRO: (a) the urban services which use vehicles
with two doors, common seats, tumstiles, and can circulate with standing passengers; and (b) the
intercity services whose buses have reclining seats and which can not circulate with standing
passengers. In 1995 there were 106 licensed bus companies regulated by DETRO. DETRO's functions
are to regulate entry/exit, tariff setting, enforce safety and monitor compliance with agreed level-of-
service. DETRO had 178 employees in 1995 of which 63 in administrative functions and 115 in operating
functions. DETRO turned an operating profit of R$3.48 million (US$3.625 million). The State will have to
continue to regulate buses and most likely DETRO will continue with SECTRAN. It is also expected that
the intermunicipal bus regulatory framework will be revamped and adapted to take into account the
recently approved concessions law.
B. Objectives of the State Reform Program in the Urban Transport Sector
13.          The objectives of the State Reform Project in the urban transport sector are: (a) To
substantially reduce the high amount of projected annual subsidies paid by the State to urban transport
agencies (estimated in 1995 at US$363 million or 10 percent of State Revenues) by a combination of
restructuring and or sales/concessions or management contracts to the private sector; (b) to provide a
better level-of-service to the users of urban transport services; (c) to finance backlog maintenance which
is affecting the capacity of the operating agencies to increase revenues and provide an acceptable level-
of-service; and (d) to introduce an air quality program for vehicle-based emissions.
14.          The proposed State Reform Project is concentrating its disbursements in actions which
will facilitate the administrative reforms and restructuring of the agencies, in studies to define the best



- 48 -                                ANNEX 4
options lFor increased private sector participation in the agencies, and in rehabilitation activities designed
to boost productivity of assets which have been immobilized due to low maintenance budgets. This is
particularly the case of Flumitrens and the Metro. Under the project, Flumitrens launched a staff
downsizing program and will improve its revenues by a combination of infrastructure and equipment
rehabilitation and reduction of fare evasion. The Metro also started to downsize its staff and will take
advantage of the completion of its BNDES-financed extension to increase its revenues. CTC and Serve,
which are the only state-owned bus companies in SRJ will have their assets sold to the private sector
and will be liquidated. Their routes will be absorbed by municipal and intermunicipal private operators.
CODERTE, the agency in charge of exploration of terminals will also be restructured since it is felt that it
is producing far less profit than it should. Finally, CONERJ, the ferry boat company will have its assets
sold and its main lines will be operated under concessions by private operators.
15.          The project will also foster the creation of a Regional Transport Coordination
Commission in charge of coordinating all the urban transport tariff and subsidy policies in the RJMR. The
AMTU-RJ will also prioritize future investments in the region and foster integrated multimodal fares. The
AMTU-RJ will provide information to the State Regulatory Entity which will oversee regulatory aspects in
the State. The AMTU-RJ will have representatives of the State Secretary of Transport, the RJ
municipality Secretary of Transport, representatives of the other municipalities of the RJMR,
representatives of the operators and the users. In January 1996, an agreement was signed by the State
and the Municipality of RJ to create a precursor of the AMTU-RJ.
16.          By the end of the project it is expected that overall subsidies to urban transport agencies
will be brought to zero in the case of the Metro, CONERJ, CTC/SERVE and reduced by two-thirds of the
1996 buclgeted level in the case of Flumitrens. Furthermore, the State has agreed to introduce financing
mechanisms to fund urban transport operating subsidies while needed and eventually contribute for
future expansions. It is expected that one mechanism to be used will consist on a surcharge on the
Vehicle Ownership Tax which is collected by the state.
C. Implementation of Reformis
17.          The implementation reforms will focus essentially on Flumitrens and the Metro which
together are the main contributors to 80 percent of the operating deficit of the sector. The overall strategy
to be followed in both cases is the following:
(a)    Staff downsizing through voluntary and involuntary dismissals;
(b)    Reduction of salary benefits to the minimum required by law;
(c)    Acceleration of private sector involvement in operations and management of the urban
(transport companies, through concessions and/or rnanagement contracts;
(cI)   Tariff adjustments to increase revenues in line with inflation; and
(e)    Sale/lease land assets and building which belong to the agencies to increase non-
operating revenues.
18.          Flumitrens and Metro. As a top priority, SRJ has decided that both Flumitrens and the
Metro should be managed by the private sector. SRJ has set January 1997 as a deadline for starting the
long term concession of the Metro and January 1998 for the longl term concession of Flumitrens. To
make both systems more attractive to the private sector, the State has requested that both Flumitrens
and the Metro start a downsizing program since the ratio urevenue passengers per staff" is too low,
suggesting that payroll costs should be reduced by almost 50 percent or the revenue passengers
increased by as much. The initial staff reduction program was completed in May 1996 (with a reduction
of 1,800 staff in Flumitrens and 800 in Metro) and affected the administrative, engineering and planning
divisions of the agencies which are overstaffed. The State has used as a model and comparator for the
Metro, thei Santiago of Chile subway which has approximately the same length, almost twice as many
passengers at the same average tariff and has an operating surplus. For Flumitrens the model chosen as



-49-                                  ANNEX4
a comparator is the Buenos Aires Suburban rail system, which is now operated by private
concessionaires and has a productivity three times higher than Flumitrens. In addition, SRJ asked
Flumitrens to put in place a fare evasion control system to increase revenues. The State has also
instructed the agencies to evaluate the land and buildings which can be sold or leased to increase non-
operating revenues.
19.          To decide the type of private management that will be more adequate for both
companies SRJ has launched two studies to evaluate the options for private sector participation in the
companies and to prepare the bidding documents for the selected option. These studies were financed
with funds of an ongoing Bank Loan (Ln. 3633-BR) which financed the decentralization of the rail system
from Federal to State Governments. SRJ feels that the present State-appointed administrations of
Flumitrens and Metro are constrained by existing legislation from being bolder in its efforts to restructure
their companies. SRJ believes that a long-term concession of both Flumitrens and the Metro will reduce
annual subsidies and improve quality of service as it did in Buenos Aires. To make both systems more
attractive to potential private sector concessionaires, SRJ has instructed Flumitrens and Metro's
management to implement the abovementioned downsizing programs which by May 1996 had exceeded
the targets set by the Government.
20.          Concessions of the Flumitrens and Metro will likely follow the Argentinean model which
calls for bids to execute an investment plan defined by the State, propose a schedule of decreasing
operating subsidies to be paid by the State, and provide a level-of-service stipulated in the bidding
documents at a tariff set by the Government. The proponent with the lowest net present value of the cost
of investment plan plus the subsidy is the winner of the concession. The Argentinean model calls for a
schedule of subsidies which become zero after a certain date, at which time the concessionaire must
start paying the government an annual concession fee. Suburban railway concessions in Argentina were
granted for 10 years and the subway concession was granted for 20 years.
21.          It could be argued that the concessions proposed are in fact management contracts to
operate the systems and, in fact, they are. Subsidies must be paid because Government reserves the
right to set tariffs which are affordable by the low-income populations which are the main users of the
systems. Also one may ask why should the Government be paying for the investment plan and why can't
the concessionaire do so. Experience has shown that if the concessionaire must raise the money (in the
case of Flumitrens the investment plan to rehabilitate about 70 percent of its plant is estimated at about
US$373 million) the concession becomes much less attractive than other opportunities available to the
private sector which have a much higher rate of return. The main advantage of a concession as done in
Argentina is that the concessionaire has an incentive to comply with the investment plan at the lowest
cost and propose the lowest subsidy schedule to win the bid, knowing full well that if its net operating
deficit is better than the subsidy, the concessionaire will be able to keep the difference. Experience has
also shown that after a government-financed investment plan, the concessionaire will risk investing its
own resources to attract more demand and increase revenues while decreasing costs. Since the
investment plan proposed for Flumitrens is designed to rehabilitate its fleet along with its signaling and
telecom plant, one may argue that it is justified because is the minimum necessary to attract the private
sector. Otherwise, chances are that investors will shy away from such ventures because they would have
very low financial rates of return.
22.          The main difference between Flumitrens and Metro is that the latter has already secured
the funds for completing the works required to double its ridership and increase its route length from 23
to 35 km, while the former will be receiving those funds under the proposed Rio de Janeiro Mass Transit
Project which is under preparation for financing by the World Bank. The investment for the Metro
(US$298 million) came from a BNDES loan, and will be used primarily for completing works started more
than 10 years ago which were halted due to the financial situation of the State. The investment for
Flumitrens (US$373 million) will be mainly devoted to rehabilitate rolling stock, and upgrading the
signaling, telecom and stations judged necessary for the system to reach a ridership of one million
passengers per day. It is therefore important to follow up the staff downsizing and operational
improvement actions started with the State Reform project with this investment program in order to
entice the private sector to operate the system under concession.



-50-                                  ANNEX4
23.          CONERJ. The ferry boat company will have its assets sold to the highest bidder.
SECTRkN will act as the overseer of the private sector concessionaires which would operate the ferry
lines in the Guanabara and Sepetiba bays. SRJ has set a date of November 1996 for completing this
sale and has commissioned a valuation study using its own funds.
24.          CTC and SERVE are also to be liquidated. Its assets are mainly buses and garages
which will be sold to the private sector. The routes operated by C(TC and SERVE will be taken over by
existing bus concessionaires which will be selected through competitive bidding.
25.          CODERTE, the agency in charge of the terminals and parking garages of the State, will
also be sold. SRJ has decided to undertake a study to determine the value of the assets involved, to
obtain aill the legal titles of the properties, and to prepare terms of reference to select a real estate
company which will sell or rent the properties owned by SRJ.
D. Sector Reforms
26.          Before awarding concessions to the private sector in the urban transport sector it is
important to define a basic sector regulatory framework and the responsibilities of each of the urban
transport agencies involved. We depart form the premise that the State has agreed to the creation of an
independient State Regulatory Commission which will oversee all the major regulatory issues in the
State, including urban transport. This commission will have a transport division, but will use a pool of
economists, lawyers and other specialists which will be dealing with all the sectors. It is also assumed
that this commission will issue basic regulatory guidelines which are to be followed in all sectors, but will
expect that each subsector will approve a set of very specific regulatory policies for each of its
subsectors. For example, based on general principles for market entry/exit, tariff setting, safety
regulations, etc. the Secretary of Transport (SECTRAN) must prepare a set of regulations for rail, sea
and land transport. These regulations must be approved by the State Regulatory Commission and serve
as the regulatory framework for the mode. SECTRAN has invited consultants to prepare such regulatory
framework and a plan to restructure SECTRAN's organization.
27.          In parallel with the preparation of a regulatory framework, it is also necessary to establish
the new structure for SECTRAN. In fact the present structure of SECTRAN and its different agencies
(Flumitrens, Metro, CONERJ, CTC/SERVE, CODERTE) will be inadequate once the new concessions to
operate those modes are granted. As mentioned in the main text, regulation of all sectors will be the
responsibility of a State Regulatory Commission. It is expected, however, that the State will delegate
inspection and monitoring and supervision functions in urban transport to SECTRAN, which in turn will
either delegate them to its directorates or to the agencies in charge of overseeing the concessionaires
day-to-day performance. To undertake this work it is expected that SECTRAN will use contractors
instead of force account. In the specific case of buses which involves the monitoring and supervision of
more than 1000 lines, SECTRAN intends to restructure DETRO to make this organization more
accountable, flexible and transparent probably by sub-contracting its functions to the private sector.
28.          At present the regulations for rail transport are fedieral regulations because until recently
the railway mode was basically under federal jurisdiction. With the decentralization of urban railways to
the State, it might be necessary to get Federal government approval for Railway State legislation.
Similarly, all sea regulation, such as the regulations that apply to CONERJ, are under the federal
jurisdiction. Again with the concession of CONERJ lines to private entrepreneurs the State may want to
get federal approval for specific State legislation. If by any chance that becomes difficult because
several lobbies may want to keep the regulation under the Federal Government umbrella, than the State
should examine other avenues to do so.
29.          Finally, the Regional Transport Coordination Commission will act as a policy coordinator
for the three levels of govemment involved in urban transport, as an adviser on tariff and subsidy policy
and a main forum to discuss and prioritize the main investments in the RJMR. It will probably be called to
make presentations to the State Regulatory Commission but will not have deliberative power.



- 51-                                 ANNEXS
BRAZIL
RIO DE JANEIRO MASS TRANSIT PROJECT
ANNEX 5
INFRASTRUCTURE AND EQUIPMENT COMPONENT
1.           General. The Flumitrens system is organized into five corridors: D. Pedro Il-Deodoro,
Deodoro-S. Cruz, Deodoro-Japeri, D. Pedro Il-B. Roxo, and B. Maua-Saracuruna, with about 684 km of
main line track. The system is electrified and passenger services are provided with electric multiple units.
The rail network mainline is broad gauge (1.60m), and has an adequate alignment, about 2 percent of
total track length is in curves with less than 300m radius (Table 5.1), permitting if the track was
maintained in good conditions, maximum speeds up to 80 km/h. Passenger traffic lines are laid mostly
with 57 kg/m rail (Table 5.2). Sleepers are impregnated timber (about 69 percent of total number placed
in mainline track), but mono-block and bi-block concrete sleepers have been used on main line relaying
for some years (Table 5.3). Rail fastenings are rigid in some sections and elastic in others. Ballast is
crushed stone of reasonable quality, but not adequate in quantity and in general, polluted. Flumitrens'
main problem related to track infra/superstructure is still deferred maintenance, the track condition has
been gradually deteriorating during the last years as a consequence of budgetary restrictions. This
situation is gradually improving as a result of the track rehabilitation works being carried out under the
ongoing Rio de Janeiro Metropolitan Transport Decentralization Project (Loan 3633-BR), works which are
expected to be completed during 1997.
2.           Flumitrens' specifications for track rehabilitation are summarized as follows: (a) rails are
standard 57 kg/m and are welded by the flush-butt process at a fixed depots into longer lengths (240m)
before laying and are then joined again in the track using the alumino-thermit process; (b) rail fastening
are of elastic type, to be used in all track renewals; (c) timber sleepers are of selected wood and
impregnated; (d) concrete sleepers are locally manufactured, pre- or post-tensioned and are placed at
1,850 per km using elastic fastenings; and (e) crushed stone ballast is provided at about 1,500m  per km.
3.           In early 1991, CBTU restructured the organization of the Rio de Janeiro System
(Flumitrens), including the permanent way labor force, decentralizing the administration by corridors. It is
felt, however, that further refinements are still needed for productivity improvements, and for allowing
private participation in track maintenance tasks and thus continue to reduce its staff. In addition, the
planning, programming, cost accounting and monitoring methods and procedures need to be
strengthened. A specific study (man-power development rationalization) is being carried out under Loan
3633-BR. The track maintenance force in Flumitrens is divided into two sectors: one responsible for the
maintenance of Deodoro, Japeri and Santa Cruz corridors and other for Belford Roxo, Gramacho and
Meter Gauge corridors. Current total working staff is about 324 staff (a reduction of 536 staff compared
to 860 in 1986), representing 0.52 staff/km, compatible with modern standards (0.40/0.50 staff/km). Each
unit has the responsibility for periodic routine maintenance in their sections of track, including the cyclical
work performed annually by special gangs with mechanized equipment. Major rehabilitation or new track
works are carried out by the private sector. The standards of maintenance can be assessed regularly on
the graphs produced by a track condition recording unit; the system has one Permanent Way workshop
in Deodoro which provides maintenance support to track equipment. The condition of the equipment fleet
is gradually improving under the rehabilitation program of the ongoing Loan 3633-BR and State Reform
Program.
A. Civil Works
4.           Civil Works under the proposed project include the rehabilitation and modemization of
eleven stations and the construction of two stations. Details of location and type of works to be
performed on those stations under the project are shown in Figure 5.1 of this Annex. This component
also includes the construction of five pedestrian overpasses, 8,000m of fences in areas adjacent to some



- 52 -                                ANNEX5
selected stations. The overall cost of this component amounts to US$31 millions including contingencies
or 8.2 percent of total project cost.
5.           Permnanent Way Rehabilitation Works. These works would consist of rail replacement
with long welded rails on 10 kms of the Leopoldina corridor, superstructure for the Gramacho-Saracuruna
second line, installation of 50,000 sleepers on the five corridors,, and would amount to about US$6.7
million including contingencies or 1.8 percent of total project cost.
B. Systems
6.           A description of the systems currently operated by Flumitrens and improvements
included in the project are summarized bellow:
(a)    Electrification. The EMU's pick up power at 3 kv DC from an overhead catenary, which
is supplied by rectifier substations at 44 kv AC input. In addition to providing power for
the trains, the substations also reduce the AC voltage to 6.6 and 4.4 kv for use in other
power facilities connected to the substations by ovierhead transmission lines. Several of
these transmission lines require upgrading because of deterioration and undersized
conductors. The proposed project includes the following interventions, in order to provide
more reliable power supply for train control systems operation:
*     installation of 4.4 kv, 44 kv and 3 Ikv transmission lines, from Gramacho
to Saracuruna and reform of the 4.4 kv and 44 kv circuits between
Barao de Maui and Gramacho;
*     installation of second circuit plus 44kv between D.Pedro II and
Mangueira;
*     rebuilding of the substation of Deodoro, currently out of operation due to
damage by fire. The equipment reqjuired are in storage;
*     rebuilding of the substation of Benfica, deteriorated because of age,
including project, with equipment that are in storage;
*     construction and installation of the substation of Saracuruna;
*     rehabilitation of the substation of Nilopolis (required equipment is
already in storage); and
*     replacement, including a study, oI the existing earth systems, at each
substation and mid point section cabin along the overhead catenary, in
view of the deterioration due to corrosion.
These improvements will reduce the interference with the main line
power flow, and increase the reliability of the system operation. This component
has been estimated to cost US$23.8 million including contingencies or 6.4
percent of total project cost.
(b)    Signaling/Train Control.
Centralized Traffic Control (CTC). Some sections of the Flumitrens system
have been equipped with the CTC components and have been made
operational. Other sections have not been equipped, and therefore the CCO
operators have to use radio and telephone lines to wayside signal locations. The
lack of CTC increases the number of operating staff required, and also has an



- 53 -                                ANNEX5
adverse effect on operating reliability. The proposed project component would
provide CTC facilities throughout the system, by adding equipment in Japeri and
Santa Cruz corridors, and in D. Pedro and Deodoro yards in order to make the
entire system operable by CTC. Some components are in the storage
warehouse, and others would be procured under the project.
Automatic Train Control (ATC). Currently, none of the EMU's fleet are
equipped with the necessary on-board cab signaling equipment, nor is any of the
field equipment (at trackside) installed. There are 180 sets of on-board
equipment stored in the warehouse, but this quantity is considered to be
insufficient to equip the entire fleet of refurbished EMU's. In addition, there is a
large quantity of ATC field equipment storage in the warehouse, and more fully-
compatible components would need to be procured under the proposed project.
The proposed project includes the installation of field and board ATC system, so
62 sets of on-board equipment have to be acquired in addition. The lack of ATC
has resulted in many near collisions between trains, and also resulted in
collisions causing damages. This lack effectively prevents headway reduction,
thus limiting the total passenger-carrying capacity of the system. The signaling
component has been estimated at US$34.8 million including contingencies or
9.4 percent of total project cost.
(c)    Telecommunications. Until now the fiber-optic cable links and the associated
multiplexing equipment were installed between D. Pedro II and Santa Cruz. The
proposed project includes the installation of this equipment on the Deodoro - Japeri line.
The necessary fiber-optic cables are in storage. This fiber-optic network, when
completed, will be used to: control and monitor the power substations and power
switches along the line; the CTC (signaling system) and the telephone links between
CCO and all substations; CTC (signal) control locations; and administration facilities
such as workshops and offices, with the CCO.
(d)    Central Control Office (CCO). The fiber-optic links carry information, data and voice
telecommunications to the CCO. The proposed project includes the connection of the
various power and CTC (signaling) locations along the line with the CCO. The
information are provided at control desks and displayed by visual indications on control
panels. All this equipment is in place and is in partial operation awaiting connection.
Some modifications to the display panels are needed to bring them into correspondence
with the revised layouts in the D. Pedro II and Deodoro yards. The overall cost of this
component amounts to US$7.5 million including contingencies or 2 percent of total
project cost.
(e)    Automatic Fare Collection. Installation of automatic fare collection system  in all
stations. This equipment has been estimated at US$11.7 million including contingencies
or 3.2 percent of total project cost.
C. Rolling Stock
7.           Attached as Table 5.4 is the actual fleet size for Rio de Janeiro showing current train
availability. Table 5.5 shows the future plan for train availability for Rio de Janeiro after implementation
of the rehabilitation proposal. These tables show substantial anticipated increases in train availability
after implementation of the proposed project component. The proposal presented to achieve this,
described in this Annex, is straightforward, feasible and technically sound. Flumitrens would have to
contract out all the work proposed due to lack of current capacity within their own organization. The work
contents of the proposal is such that it is expected that a wide range of capable contractors could submit
competitive bids. The contractors could be local or international or a combination involving Joint
Ventures or Licensing Agreements. The overall work content entail mechanical, electrical and civil



-54-                                   ANNEX5
engineering. The extent of these works, coupled with the quantities involved, is expected to be attractive
to interested Contractors. Within the Rio de Janeiro network there are several passenger train sets
presently not available for revenue service due to the following reasons (Table 5.4): (a) Accident/collision
damage; (b) lack of spare parts due to budgetary restrictions; and (c) material/components
cannibalization, resulting from (b). These factors have significantly reduced the number of train sets for
passenger service use (train availability). An ideal service should provide between 90 percent and 92
percent availability, the remaining 8 percent to 10 percent is out cf service for maintenance. The existing
train sets are in average condition, and achieve about 60 percent service availability. By investing in the
proposed rehabilitation and modernization, the network would raise train availability figures significantly
in order to cope with the projected passenger demand (Table 5.5).
8.            The proposed rolling stock component, which is the largest included in the project, is
designed to improve passenger train set availability and reliability to meet the planned future increased
revenue service. It covers the following areas: (a) rehabilitation of 103 EMU's currently operating; (b)
Refurbishment/modernization of 36 EMU's currently out of service; and (c ) rehabilitation of bogies and
pneumatic systems of 44 EMUs. The cost of this component has been estimated at US$245 million
including contingencies or 65 percent of total project cost. The main characteristics of the EMU's fleet to
be rehabilitated under this project component are:
For rehabilitation: Total - 103
Carbon steel - 34 EMU Series 400 - 35 years (modernized 19 years ago)
Stainless steel - 2 EMU Series 500 - 19 years
Stainless steel - 25 EMU Series 700 - 16 years
Stainless steel - 10 EMU Series 800 - 16 years
Stainless steel - 32 EMU Series 900 - 16 years
For refurbishment/modernization: Total - 36
Carbon steel - 6 EMU Series 400 - 35 years (modernized 19 years ago)
Stainless steel - 3 EMU Series 500 - 19 years
Stainless steel - 4 EMU Series 700 - 16 years
Stainless steel - 7 EMU Series 800 - 16 years
Stainless steel - 16 EMU Series 900 - 16 years
9.            Engineering  Designs. Final engineering designs for track rehabilitation works,
modernization of nine stations and specifications for part olF the EMU's fleet rehabilitation and
refurbishment program  have been completed. Flumitrens plans to finish by September 1996 all
preliminary engineering designs as well as final engineering desiigns for those components included in
the first year of the project.
10.          Supervision. The project includes the provision of consultants (1,324 stafflmonths) for
the implementation and supervision of each project component. The cost of these services have been
estimated at US$6 million including contingencies or 1.5 percent of total project cost.

BRAZIL
RIO DE JANEIRO MASS TRANSIT PROJECT
ANNEX 6
ENVIRONMENTAL AND TRAFFIC SUB-COMPONENT
A. Background
1.           This section describes the environmental and traffic safety component of the proposed
project which includes:
(a)   the design of a vehicle inspection and maintenance program; and
(b)   the development of a system to process and collect traffic violation fines and
enforce them as a deterrent to traffic accidents.
2.           In addition, the proposed project includes studies and technical assistance to support
improvements in motor vehicle emissions control and the development of an integrated
transport/environmental strategy for the RJMR.
B. The Environmental Sub-Component
3.           Inspection and maintenance (I/M) serves two purposes in a vehicle emissions control
program. First, it helps to identify vehicles in which maladjustment or other mechanical problems are
causing higher emissions than necessary. Second, it ensures that the emission control equipment on
vehicles so equipped remains in place and operational, so that the emissions benefits of that equipment
are realized. The first purpose applies mostly to older vehicles without emission controls; for emission-
controlled vehicles, the second is much more important. An effective I/M program is essential to ensure
that the emissions benefits projected for the adoption of new vehicle emissions standards are actually
attained. The project finances the design of an I/M program and will recommend how the l/M program
can be implemented through concessions to the private sector. The cost of the basic study to design the
l/M program is estimated at US$0.6 million.
C. The Traffic Subcomponent
4.           The objective of the traffic management component is to design and put in place a
system to enforce, process and collect fines for traffic violations. This study is estimated to cost US$0.4
million. The number of traffic violations in the RJMR is high, but once fined, traffic offenders do not pay
their fines. Because the system to process and collect fines is very slow, traffic violators continue to
disrespect regulations and are not properly checked. The proceeds of fines would also contribute to funds
for the refinement of the urban transport system.

BRAZIL
RIG DE JANEIRO MASS TRANSIT PROJECT
ANNEX 7
INSTITUTIONAL DEVELOPMENT AND POLICY STUDIES
1.           This annex describes the studies and technical assistance for project supervision and
management and training to be contracted to support the policy and institutional development
component. The studies will be divided in three categories: (a) studies which are only Flumitrens related;
and (b) studies to enhance modal integration and (c) the training component which should contribute to
the training of staff which will be monitoring the concession as well as training in-house and abroad.
2.           A list of the proposed studies and training is presented below. Draft terms of reference
for the first year studies and outlines for the other studies were agreed at appraisal and were ready by
negotiations.
A. Flumitrens Related Studies
3.           Assisting the Concession to the Private Sector. The first set of Actions Plans
concerns the preparation of bid documents to be used in the concession process: (a) institutional and
organizational arrangements; (b) investment plan to be undertaken; (c) evaluation of operating subsidies;
(d) patrimonial agreement on the valuation and assets to transferred; (e) aspects relating to transfer of
personinel, pension benefits, and miscellaneous human resource-related issues; and (f) legal and
administrative aspects for concession;
4.           Technical Assistance Until the Concession. rhis technical assistance will provide key
advisors who will work with the President of Flumitrens to further rationalize and stream line operations
and mnaintenance to decrease the perceived risks of the conceission. For example, hiring of experts in
rolling stock maintenance and railways operations management, management accounting to define clear
cost allocation rules, specialists to implement the payroll software, experts to build a reliable data base.
4.           Technical Engineering Studies in Signaling and Telecom. These studies would
address the needs identified during preparation for the installation of existing and new signaling and
telecoirn equipment.
5.           Supervision Consultants. The implementation of the investment plan will be
supervised by engineering consultants in charge of ensuring that the works and goods comply with
specifications.
6.           Project Management Consultants. These consultants will be in charge of supporting the
Project Management and Implementation Group until the completion of the Project.
B. Modal Integration Related Studies
7.           Modal Integration. The objective of the second set of Action Plans is to support the
establishment of the AMTU-RJ policy framework and provide practical recommendations for
implenmenting modal integration, namely: (a) updating of the Transport, Land Use, and Air Quality portion
of the Master Plans of the RJMR with emphasis on route rationailization and modal and tariff integration;
and (b) a intermunicipal route management and rationalization study.
8.           Niter6i - Sao Gongalo Corridor. Preparation of feasibility studies and bidding
documents for the Niter6i - S3o Gon,alo mass transportation system. the RJMR master plan should be
analyzed in order to give the basic guidelines for the development of future systems for this corridor.

functions: (a) to manage the implementation of the investment plan; and (b) to monitor the
concessionaire on behalf of the state regulatory entity.

- 69 -                                ANNEX 9
BRAZIL
RIO DE JANEIRO MASS TRANSIT PROJECT
ANNEX 9
ECONOMIC EVALUATION
A. Background
1.           A standard cost-benefit analysis was performed to evaluate the economic impact of the
implementation of the proposed infrastructure and equipment component (consolidate the rehabilitation
of Flumitrens and build/rehabilitate the transfer stations between the trains, metro and ferries), which
represents 96 percent of the total proposed project. This Annex describes the demand analysis and the
set of benefits and costs that have been considered in the economic evaluation, as well as the
assumptions adopted regarding key inputs, such as the value of time. Direct user benefits include travel
time and operating cost savings; indirect benefits arise from the reduction of negative externalities
(accident and air pollution costs). Investment costs comprise civil works, permanent way, operating
systems, rolling stock, and costs of projects and studies. Both benefits and costs are defined as
incremental compared to the situation of the end of the project CBTU/IBRD I (Ln. 3633-BR). Throughout
the analysis, and for illustrative purposes, the proposed investments were evaluated with and without the
accident and air pollution externalities. A more detailed description of the data and assumptions
supporting the economic evaluation summarized in this annex can be found in Reports 8 and 9 in the
Project Files.
2.           This economic evaluation estimates the discounted expected present value of the
infrastructure investment's net benefits, the expected internal economic rates of return, as well as the
expected benefit/cost ratios for a set of discount rates, which includes the 10 percent discount rate, which
has been defined by SEAIN (Secretary of Planning for the Federative Republic of Brazil), as well by its
Commission of External Financing (COFIEX) for the project appraisal of foreign investments. In addition,
the sensitivity analysis included in this annex provides an idea of how the economic rates of return would
change due to deviations from the value of benefits and costs used in the base case. The analysis is
then complemented by a table of impacts of the project which are evaluated on a qualitative basis.
B. Choice of a Project Base Case
Mass Transit Options
3.           Following current Bank policy recommendations for the appraisal of mass transit
investments contained in the Urban Transport Policy Paper (World Bank, 1986), the proposed
investments were compared against two totally segregated busways with regular buses and a totally
segregated busways with articulated buses (Annex 13, ref. 8a). In all cases, the analyses showed that the
proposed investments had a higher internal rate of return.
C. Demand Analysis
Demand Modeling
4.           The demand estimates used in the economic evaluation are the result of a simulation of
the Rio de Janeiro Metropolitan Region transport network, performed with the Mantra System'
microcomputer program. The urban transport demand model used by this program is a classical
aggregate four-stage transport planning model, which comprises trip generation, trip distribution, modal
Mantra Computer System was developed by Logit - Logistica Informatica e Transportes, Brazil, and SDG - Steer
Davies Gleave, England.



-70-                                  ANNEX 9
split, and trip assignment. This model estimates peak (6 hours) and off-peak (10 hours) periods of public
transport demand, which are assigned to bus, metro and railway networks.
5.           The model Mantra is integrated with a Geographic Information System, GisPlus. 2 In
order to use the model, it was necessary to create a GIS database for Rio de Janeiro Metropolitan
Region (RJMR), which comprises a zoning layer and a transport network layer (made up of line and node
layers). To develop the databases for the demand study, the team benefited from the existence of a
model of the transport network prepared under the Transport Master Plan of the RJMR financed by the
Bank. This network was prepared using GisPlus and then imported by Mantra.
6.           The zoning system adopted is based on the RJ sub-regions, which were defined in the
Rio de Janeiro Master Plan. This zoning system was digitizecl using GisPlus and then imported by
Mantra.
7.           The present demand study used as primary data the 1994 Household  and
Origin/Destination (OD) survey undertaken by IPLAN-RIO for the Rio de Janeiro Master Plan based on
household samples in all RJMR. From this survey were obtained the following data: transport demand on
a daily/hourly basis, breakdown into peak and off-peak period ancd breakdown into trip purposes.
8.           Peak hour periods considered were the following: 06:00 a.m. to 09:00 a.m. and 16:00
p.m. to 19:00 p.m. The number of trips in this period corresponds to 56,4 percent of the weekday
ridershiip. Both periods have similar number of trips and almost the same peak hour factor. The OD
matrix breakdown into trip purposes shows that 56,6 percent of trips are commuter and business trips
and 43,4 percent are related to other trip purposes.
9.           The steps in the demand modeling exercise were:
(a)    Trip Generation. This step consists of the estimation of the number of trips
which will be produced by and attracted to the area of influence of the projects
considered in the study. In the present study it was conservatively assumed that
the 1994 OD matrix is a good estimate up to the year 2001. After this year, a
yearly demand increase of 1 percent per year was considered. Car and walking
trips were not considered at all.
(b)    Trip Distribution. This step consists of the determination of the origin zone and
destination zone for each trip based on the types of trips produced by and
attracted to each zone and the interzonal "impedance" values (typically travel
times). Also in this case there is the assumption of using the current matrix as an
estimate up to the year 2001.
(c)    Modal Choice consists of the calculation of which trips will use the bus network
and the metro system. The Mantra model allows the competition between single
modes and combined modes. The modail choice model used is the multinomial
Logit model. It is important to point out that for each zone pair of interest, for the
area of influence of the Railway lines, there will always be competition between
bus and railway+bus and in some cases metro+bus. The model utility functions
considered the same values of travel time used in the economic evaluation. Non
cash cost of transfer is taken into account as an impedance to choose a mode.
(d)    Trip assignment consists of the "loadinig" of the passenger trips onto the multi-
modal public transport network. The method used to assign the trips made within
each model, is the "all or nothing" procedure with no capacity restraint iterations
2     GisPlus is a Geographic Information System developed by Caliper Ccrporation, USA.



- 71 -                                ANNEX 9
for bus and incremental assignment with capacity restraint for the railway and
metro lines.
10.          The model was applied for the situation "without" and "with" the project. The outputs
obtained from the application of the model are the number of trips, passenger-kms and passenger-hours
by each individual mode: bus, metro and railway. By running the model for the situation "without" and
"with," data were obtained on the additional trips in the railway mode, and also the decrease in bus trips
which result from the implementation of the proposed project. The following paragraphs summarize the
situation "without" and "with" project for the railway lines above and the results in terms of passenger-
kilometers, passenger-hours and average speeds are summarized below:
(a)    Situation 'Without Project" (Year 1998 and 2001). This is the situation at the
end of CBTU/BIRD I project (Ln. 3633-BR). The main traffic characteristics of
the study area in the situation "without" the project on a daily basis are: average
peak-hour bus speeds of 17.04 km/hour with a total of 4,094,360 passengers,
with 83,594,816 passenger-kms generated by bus trips with origins or
destinations in the RJMR. The corresponding number of passenger hours is
5,005,279. The railway trips generated for daily origins/destinations in the study
area a total of 837,955 passengers with 20,979,434 passenger-kms,
corresponding to 517,063 passenger-hours. The transit modal choice in terms of
trips generated by the study area would be 71.75 percent bus, 13.56 percent
metro, and 14.69 percent railway.
(b)    Situation "With Project" (Year 1998). On a daily basis, the average peak-hour
bus speeds in the study area would rise to 17.66 km/h and daily demand for bus
transportation would go down to 3,954,717 passengers with 80,778,031
passenger-kms and 4,845,248 passenger-hours. The daily demand for railway
trips will go up to a total of 945,315 passengers with 23,707,032 passenger-kms
and 584,492 passenger-hours. The transit modal choice in terms of trips
generated by the study area
would be 70.05 percent bus,    Table 9.1: Demand Levels in the Railway

16.74 percent railway.      Item                          Amount

11.          The demand estimates for the railway lines (all corridors) in the year 2001 indicate: (a) a
peak-hour inbound volume of about 93,847 passengers; (b) a peak-hour outbound volume of 31,881
passengers; (c) a total of about 8,673,012 week-day boardings; and (d) an annual ridership of more than
450 million passengers.
D. Benefits
Value ofl Time
12.          Mass transit projects can hardly be economically justified without accounting for the real
resource savings that economies of time represent. The monetary value of time by which the time
savings ,are multiplied to obtain the benefits from time savings is a key input in the economic evaluation
of the infrastructure components of this project. Individual values of time are traditionally considered to
be a function of income, in particular a function of the individual wage level. Thus, the value of time used
in the calculation of time savings is derived from the income distribution of users, disaggregated by
transport mode and trip purpose, and from information on wage levels. Table 9.2 shows the income of
public transport users in the zone of influence of the FLUMITRENS and is disaggregated by mode. The
information was obtained from the 1994 Household and Origin/Destination (OD) survey for the Rio de
Janeiro MAaster Plan.
13.          The base minimum wage used was R$112 which at the shadow price of foreign
exchange of 1 is equivalent to US$112. From this minimum wage base, the value of fringe benefits was
considered separate from payroll taxes. Fringe benefits in Brazil include vacation and the 13th month of
salary; both entail scaling the base salary by a factor of 1.25. Commuting and other purpose trips are
valued as a proportion of net wages. This implies subtracting direct taxes paid by the employee from the
base wage, which includes fringe benefits. The employee is subject to a payroll tax that varies between 8
percent and 10 percent. In addition, the income tax should be subtracted. By assuming that one-third of
passengers are not tax-exempt and do not evade taxes, the net wage deflating factor will be in the 0.92-
0.88 range. An average factor of 0.9 was used. Business trips are valued at gross wages, where fringe
benefits and payroll taxes are taken into account. Payroll taxes in Brazil vary between 38 percent and 45
percent and about 35 percent are paid by the employer. By combining fringe benefits and employer
contribution on payroll taxes, the base wage must be scaled by a factor of 1.60 (1.25 plus 0.35).
14.          Hourly wages for each transport mode users are estimated by multiplying the individual
wage (in minimum wages) by the value of the minimum wage and dividing the result by 157, the
assumed average number of hours worked in a given month. Common practice in the appraisal of urban
transport projects is to use a proportion of the hourly wage rate to approximate the value of time saved.
This propiortion as well as the wage rate base chosen for the calculation-whether gross or net wages-
depend on the type of trip. Three types of trip can be distinguished for the purposes of the analysis:
home-to-work trips (or commuting trips), work-related trips (or business trips), and non-work related trips
(or other trips), for example shopping or visiting the doctor. E-conomic theory indicates that both
commuting trips and other trips should be valued as a proportion of the net wage. Empirical evidence
suggests that this proportion may vary between 20 and 100 percent. The value adopted in the economic
evaluation was a value of 17.5 percent based on a study undertaken a few years ago for a similar city
with the :same type of population. A 20 percent value was tested as part of the sensitivity analysis.
Economic theory also indicates that business trips during working hours be valued at 100 percent of the
gross wage rate.3 The resulting values of time used in the economic evaluation are summarized in Table
9.5 and the proportion of passenger-hours spent traveling by type of trip is summarized in Table 9.3.
When the! value of time is equal to zero the IERR is still 38.17 percent.
In developed countries, estimates of the value of time for journey to work ranges from 20 to 100 percent of the
gross wage rate. A recent review of the literature concludes that a reasonable average value of time for journey to
work is 50 percent of the gross wage rate. See Kenneth Small, Urban Transportation Economics, Harwood
Academic Publishers 1992; page 44.



- 73 -                                ANNEX 9
Direct Benefits
15.          Direct benefits estimated in the economic evaluation are travel time savings, operating
cost savings, bus system control savings, and road maintenance cost savings:
(a)    Travel time savings result mainly from the transfer of bus passengers into the
new lines, and from existing metro and rail passengers who can save time in
their trips by taking advantage of the new transport facility. Travel time savings
also result from the increased operating speeds of the buses that continue
operation and, to a lesser extent, of car traffic. The program used to model the
demand works with an origin-destination matrix where the number of trips is
fixed. The number of "legs" comprising those trips may vary. When the new link
is introduced in the network, the modal split is affected and some trip itineraries
change. Thus, the demand model estimates the diverted demand, but it does
not estimate the so-called generated demand, that is, the additional trips that
will be potentially generated throughout the RJMR, but particularly in the
corridors directly affected by the investment. Omission of generated demand
yields a conservative level of demand and, consequently, results in a lower rate
of retum. Travel time savings are measured for each mode by the difference
between the total number of moming peak passenger-hours spent without the
project and those spent with the project. These peak-hour estimates are
converted to annual values and then multiplied by the assumed value of time to
obtain a monetary value of time savings. The net change in travel time across
the four modes is considered to be the overall measure of the travel time
savings;
{lPass-hr (w/o) - Pass-hr (w)] x VoT x PHF x ND)} 1,000, where Pass-hr are
passenger-hours, VoT is the value of time, PHF is the peak-hour factor, and ND
is the number of operating days in a year.
(b)    Operating cost savings for the non-rail modes derive principally from the
improved commercial and traffic speeds, which can be achieved by buses and
cars, respectively. Operating cost savings are measured for each mode by the
difference between the moming peak passenger-kilometers carried without the
project and those carried with the project. All trips were considered irrespective
of their purpose. These peak-hour estimates are converted to annual values and
then multiplied by the assumed values of operating cost per passenger-kilometer
for each mode to obtain a monetary value of operating cost savings. The net
change in operating costs across the four modes is considered to be the overall
measure of operating cost savings. Operating cost savings include wages and
salaries, materials, energy and general expenditures. An alternative method to
estimate these savings was to calculate the actual number of buses eliminated
at peak hour due to the construction of the line and estimate the savings due to
the elimination of the buses and reduction in bus kilometers produced. Table 9.4
shows the calculations used.
{[Pass-km (w/o) x OC (w/o)] - [Pass-km (w) x OC (w)] x PHF x ND}, 1,000,
where OC are operating costs per passenger-kilometer.
(c)    Bus system control cost savings depend critically on the reduction of the bus
vehicle fleet, which is assumed to follow from the reduction in bus patronage
after the metro line opens. Bus system control cost savings are measured by a
given percentage (8 percent) of bus operating costs.



-74 -                                 ANNEX9
(d)    Road maintenance cost savings derive from the possibility of delaying
periodic maintenance mainly because of reduced bus traffic on the corridors
under consideration. The benefits from reduced vehicle operating costs resulting
from reduced pavement damage are not considered.
{[Pass-km (w/o) - Pass-km (w)] x RMC, x PHF x ND}, 1,000 where RMC are
road maintenance cost savings per passenger-kilometer.
Indirect Benefits
16.          Indirect benefits arise from the reduction in externalities, such as accidents and air
pollution:
(a)    Accident cost savings are measured for each mode by the difference between
the moming peak passenger-kilometers carried without the project and those
carried with the project. These peak-hour estimates are converted to annual
values and then multiplied by the assuimed values of accident costs per 1,000
passenger-kilometer for each mode to obtain a monetary value of accident cost
savings. The net change in accident costs across the four modes is considered
to be the overall measure of accident cost savings. Accident costs include
property damage, injuries and loss of life.
{[(Pass-km (w/o)),1,000 - (Pass-km (w)X)1,000J x ACC x PHF x ND)  1,000,
where ACC are accident cost savings per 1000 passenger kilometer.
(b)    Air pollution cost savings are measured for bus and cars by the difference
between the moming peak passenger-kilometers carried without the project and
those carried with the project. These peak-hour estimates are deflated by the
average vehicle occupancy (to obtaiin costs per vehicle-kilometer) then
converted to annual values, and finally multiplied by the assumed values of air
pollution costs per vehicle-kilometer to obtain a monetary value of air pollution
cost savings. The air pollution factor (US$/gram) of emission reduction)
represents the total health and welfare benefits associated with the reduced
pollution emissions resulting from the proposed investments. Health benefits
include reduced morbidity and mortality whereas welfare benefits include the
reductions in soiling and corrosion of buildings and other structures. The net
change in air pollution costs in the bus and car modes is considered to be the
overall measure of air pollution cost savings. The set of air pollutants considered
include carbon monoxide (CO), hydrocarbons (HC), nitrogen oxides (NOX),
sulphur oxides (SOx) and fine particulate matter (PM1 0).
{([Pass-km (w/o) - Pass-km (w)] x APC x PHF x ND) AOF)}, 1,000 x where
APC are air pollution cost savings per passenger-kilometer and AOF is the
average vehicle occupancy factor.

18.          Operating Costs. The incremental operating costs due to the investments are wages
and salaries, materials, energy and general expenditures (e.g., auditing, legal costs, transport, and
cleaning services).
19.          Discount Rate. In calculating the net present value for the underground and rail
integration components of the project a discount rate must be selected which represents the opportunity
cost of capital in Brazil. The discount rate used as "benchmarkN by SEAIN (Secretary of Planning) for
foreign investments, as well as COFIEX (Commission of Extemal Financing) is 10 percent.
F. Results of Economic Evaluation
20.          Internal economic rates of return (IERR), net present values (NPV), and Benefit/Cost
ratios (B/C) were calculated. The assumed project life is 25 years. Residual values were estimated at the
end of the project life for the civil works, permanent way, operating systems and rolling stock. These
residual values were calculated following FLUMITRENS accounting procedures that assume a linear
depreciation rate, which vary according to the expected operational life of the components.
Distribution of Costs and Benefits
21.          Table 9.6 shows the annual series of expected benefits and costs for the proposed
project in the base case scenario.
Net Present Values
22.          The discounted expected present value of benefits for both investments net of costs, are
positive in the relevant range, which includes the discount rate of 10 percent are given in Table 9.6.
Intemal Economic Rates of Return
23.          The estimated IERR for the base case is given in Table 9.6.
First-year Benefit Test
24.          A "first-year" benefit test was performed to assess whether the investments are
premature. Table 9.10 show the results of the test for discount rates of 10 percent and 12 percent. For
both discount rates the proposed investments have benefits larger than the rate of discount multiplied by
the net present value of total investment costs in the planned year of start-up of operations. This
suggests that there are no additional net benefits to be gained if the implementation of the project is
delayed from the schedule assumed in the base case scenario.
Sensitivity Analysis and Switching Values
25.          A sensitivity analysis was performed to estimate the variance of the IERR in relation to
the calculated value (base case) if deviations occur in the major benefit and cost components (travel
time savings, operating cost savings, wages and salaries, general expenditures, investment costs). Table
9.7 shows the results. The switching values are shown at Table 9.8. The cases tested in the sensitivity
analysis of the financial evaluation were tested also and the results are shown at Table 9.9.
Results by Corridor
26.          An economic evaluation by corridor was performed to investigate the IERR of all
corridors. Table 9.11 shows the IERR, NPV and BenefiVCost ratios (B/C) for each corridor for the base
case, best case and worst case.
Qualitative Analysis of the Infrastructure and Equipment Component
27.          Table 9.12 is an impact matrix which summarizes the impact of the proposed investment
on a number of aspects which could not be quantified in the standard benefit cost analysis.

-87-                                 ANNEX 10A
BRAZIL
RIO DE JANEIRO MASS TRANSIT PROJECT
ANNEX IOA
FINANCIAL EVALUATION
1.           Past Financial Performance. The Companhia Fluminense de Trens Urbanos
(Flumitrens) was, formed as a separate company fully owned by the State of Rio de Janeiro on December
22, 1994. Previous to the formation of Flumitrens, the suburban railway services in the Rio de Janeiro
Metropolitan Region (RJMR) were provided by Companhia Brasilera de Trens Urbanos (CBTU), a
company of the Federal Government. As part of the decentralization policy for the suburban railways, the
Federal Government restructured the Balance Sheet of Flumitrens thereby absorbing all outstanding
liabilities while agreeing to finance the ongoing investment program aimed at expanding the system's
capacity (CBTU/BNDES Program)4. Under CBTU, STU-RJ (Superintendency for Rio de Janeiro), highly
depended upon federal subsidies to meet its operating deficits, to service its debt and to undertake
capital investments. While Flumitrens, as well, would continue to depend on external cash infusions, as
part of its own financial restructuring, the State of Rio de Janeiro has made an important decision: to
increase the efficiency of the system by concessioning it to the private sector. The operating subsidies
are, therefore, expected to fall.
2.            From a high of 940 thousand paying passengers per day reached in 1984 (CBTU took
over the system that year), the ridership has continued to drop every year. By 1995, it had dropped by 54
percent to 370 thousand passengers, a decline of 5 percent per year. The paying ridership is further
expected to decline to below 300 mark during 1996 mainly due to capacity constraints. In July 1996,
there were only 74 trains providing the service while more than 200 stood paralyzed due to lack of spare,
parts, accidents, and overhauls. The deficiencies of the system go beyond rolling stock to permanent
way, signaling, and stations. About 17 percent of the revenue is lost to evasion and another amount
estimated to be 5-10 percent is not recovered due to high level of ticket fraud.

Annual Subsidy                  (152.5)  (121.3)   (78.4)   (41.5)   (27.1)     6.7       8.0
Daily Ridership ('000)            398      523      7        967    1,128    1,367    1,400.
3.            A combination of historically low tariffs and declining ridership led to a gradual erosion of
Flumitrens' earning capacity. With about 134 million trips in 1995, Flumitrens received about US$186
million in operational subsidies that year to cover its working expenditures of US$223 million. .The
resulting working ratio was thus 6.12 and the internally generated revenues were barely enough to cover
16 percent of the working costs. Personnel expenses were responsible for about 85 percent of the
working costs with the parts and materials costing four percent of the working cost . The remainder of the
working cost covered administrative expense, energy cost, and other miscellaneous expenditures.
4.            Since the takeover of the system by the State, Flumitrens has made a strong effort to
reduce its staffing costs. Between 1995 and 1996, about 2,000 employees were let go through various
incentive programs which were financed directly by the State Government. As a result, the salary budget
is expected to decrease by 16 percent between 1995-1996. Nonetheless, the overall working costs is not
expected to go down much as the difference would be picked up by maintenance which had been
ignored until now in order to pay the salaries.
5.             Besides the staff reduction program, the Fluimitrens management is in the process of
taking other steps to rationalize payroll. Extra benefits (e.g., overtime) is expected to be reduced by 10
percent in 1997 and another 25 percent when the concessionaire takes over the system in 1998. The
responsibility for ticket sales in key stations is in the process of being passed to the private sector under
a management contract for provision of ticket salesmen (There are some examples which already exist
on Niteroi line). This is expected to greatly reduce the ticket fraud. However, the railway would have to
begin to raise revenues if it were ever to achieve financial independence. The current investment
progirams directly aim at increasing the system capacity and thus would allow Flumitrens to capture long-
distance commuter demand.
6.            Financial Projections. With the proposed irnprovement and integration of the mass
transit network, which will start by mid-1998, annual traffic is expected to reach in excess of 426 million
passengers by 2001-an increase of more than 217 percent over 1995. The operating revenue is
expected to increase four-fold over the same period to US$210 million compared to US$35 million in
1995. This expected increase in the revenue is brought about by: (a) newly generated ridership due to
increiased system capacity; (b) rationalization of general tariff structure; and (c) integration of Flumitrens
with other transport modes (metro and bus) which would result in about 15 percent of the trips. The bus
routes are expected to be redesigned in order to feed the railway system. The improved Flumitrens is
expetcted to have an advantage of 15-30 minutes over other competitive modes while maintaining below
market tariffs.
Working Costs are cash expenditures that do not include depreciation, deferred expenses, interest charges, and tax.



-89-                                ANNEX 1OA
7.            With increased and enhanced service, tariffs are generally expected to increase in order
to better reflect the true economic value of the system and thus increase the availability of internally
generated funds. However, the proposed increase in average tariff-from US$0.34 in 1996 to US$0.49 in
2001-is chiefly achieved by cutting evasion and fraud. The standard tariff itself is expected to increase
modestly-from US$0.50 in 1996 to US$0.55 in 1988-2002 (in constant 1996 US$)-due largely because
of social reasons. The major factor responsible for high farebox revenues would be an increase in the
ridership. It is important that the integrated tariffs (rail + bus, rail + metro) be competitive with the through
bus tariff in order to have a successful integration of different transport modes.
8.            Operating expenses are expected to increase after a period of slow demand in 1997-
1998. The number of staff, used as a proxy for out-sourcing, is expected to increase as a result. The
staffing estimates are generally considered conservative; it is possible that actual staffing costs
(including out sourcing) are less due to an increase in labor productivity (In the case of the railway
concessions in Argentina, labor productivity increased four-fold). Similarly, expenditure on maintenance
is expected to increase with increased car-km which are expected to double between 1996-2001.
9.           Major Financial Forecast Assumptions. The financial forecast is based on January
1996 values and uses 1995 as the base year. To develop the financial forecast, the following major
assumptions were considered in addition to Flumitrens' past financial performance.
-     Ridership is mainly constrained by capacity. As more trains are put on the system during
1996-2002, ridership is expected to grow: from 398,000 per day in 1996 to 1,400,000 per
day in 2002. Growth in revenues is expected to be above the growth in passengers due
to a drastic reduction in fraud and evasion.
Average tariff is expected to increase in real terms during 1996-2002 from US$0.34 to
US$0.49. This increase is mostly due to: (a) a reduction in the evasion - from 22
percent to 5 percent; (b) a decrease in public service commitments as a percentage of
total ridership; and (c) a small increase in the standard tariff - from US$0.50 to
US$0.55.
*     Private concessionaire is expected to take over the system as of January 1, 1998;
Non-operational income is expected to reach four percent of the operational income by
2001, including income from advertisement. These results are similar to those observed
in other rapid transit systems;
*     Personnel expenses are expected to rise by 3 percent per annum during the projection
period. This is in addition to the increase in personnel cost due to added employment.
*     Energy cost is assumed to vary directly in proportion to car-km during the projection
period.
*     Spare and material cost is expected to increase at 2.25 times more than the change in
car-km to enhance its participation in the working costs. The low level of maintenance
expenditure is the major reason for the lack of system capacity.
*     Staffing numbers were used from the Flumitrens' reorganization study. These numbers
are considered high but were used in the projections as compensation for sourcing out
the services
*     Allowances are expected to be cut by 10 percent in 1997 and another 25 percent in 1998
when the concessionaire takes over the system.



- 90 -                               ANNEX IOA
The financial analysis does not include the cost of 185 additional persons plus their
related operating expenses who will form part of Flumitrens Holding; responsible for
monitoring the Concession. The estimated costs for Flumitrens Holding are in the order
of US$6-8 million per year.
10.          Sensitivity Analysis. The financial forecast is highly dependent on the ridership
projectiions which in turn are based on the availability of train capacity. Given the past financial
constraints faced by CBTU/Flumitrens, the achievement of the financial goals is directly linked to the
ability of the State Government to concession at the agreed date. In the absence of a complete
concession, extensive private sector participation in the operations, management, and maintenance
would be required.
11.          In the worst possible case, in which the State fails to concession the system or attract
major private sector participation, the financial position of Flumitrens is not expected to improve much
over its current performance. On the other hand if there is just a delay in the concession by one year, the
State would be required to pump in an additional US$365 during the forecast period. In this particular
case, the railway is not expected to be profitable (on cash basis) until the year 2004. The biggest
concern, however, is the failure of the ridership to materialize to the levels expected, with or without the
concession. In the case that the projected demand is overstated by 25 percent, the railway would require
an additional US$238 million in operating subsidies.
12.          The railway is, however, expected to benefit frorn a reduction in its employment due to
possible out-sourcing of major services and because of morie efficient use of labor. In this case,
productivity level of labor is 25 percent higher than the forecast, the railway is expected to generate extra
cash of about US$132 million. If the tariffs were also allowed to rise in conjunction with the improvement
is the quality of service, the railway is expected to contribute to the State treasury rather than taking in
external infusions of cash. Results of the Sensitivity Analysis are shown in Table IOA.12.
13.           Financial Rate of Return. The IRR was calculated on the basis of conditions that would
exist in the absence of the proposed project (or the current conditions would continue). In doing so,
certain assumptions were made: the staff reduction program would not progress further and the level of
staff would continue to be at 5,800 which existed in July 1996. Similarly, the farebox revenues would be
lower because of: (a) persistent evasion; (b) lower tariff (at US$0.50); (c) lack of integration among
differenit modes of transport; and (d) lower production capacity. Based on these assumptions, the IRR
was calculated to be 13 percent for an investment return over the seven years; 33 percent if the return
period were increased to 10 years and 35 percent thereafter.

-104 -                                 ANNEX 10B
BRAZIL
RIO DE JANEIRO MASS TRANSIT PROJECT
ANNEX IOB
STATE FINANCES
1.            Program Seffing. The present administration, w/hich took office on January 1, 1995,
inheritecl a difficult financial situation, with large commitments to personnel, a heavy debt burden and a
major loss-making bank. The state's overextended civil service consumed 84 percent of state revenues
in 1995. State debt, excluding the liabilities of the state bank, totaled Rs.$7.3 billion, equal to 1.4 times
state revenues (all values are expressed in prices of December 1996). Of this, 70 percent consisted of
high-cost state bonds, which bore a real annual interest rate of 33 percent in 1995. The administration
responded by freezing hiring, cutting investments and taking advantage of the federal govemment's
willingness to permit interest on state bonds to be capitalized. As a result, in 1996, the state was
successlul in holding personnel costs to their 1995 level (as a percent of revenues) and maintaining
investment spending at a minimal seven percent of revenues. The state's debt stock, however,
continued to grow. Due to the capitalization of unpaid interest on state bonds, new borrowing to finance
arrears to personnel, and growth in the stock of arrears themselves, the state's total stock of debt
increased to Rs.$8.3 billion by the end of 1996.
2.            The state's recent debt agreement with the federal government (whose protocol was
initialed in January 1997) particularly affected its stock of debt and its prospects in fiscal improvement.
Under the agreement, the state's bond debt, along with certain other existing contractual debts, will be
transfomed into a 30 year debt to the federal treasury. As part of the agreement, however, the state is
required to acknowledge Rs.$6.3 billion in liabilities arising from its ownership of the state bank Banco
do Estado do Rio de Janeiro (BANERJ). This will be included in the package of refinanced debt, and will
roughly double the amount of outstanding state contractual and bonded debt. Under the agreement, the
state is required to make an upfront transfer of assets to the federal government, which will be partly
used to reduce the initial stock of debt. The state estimates the value of assets to be transferred (net of
the amount the state can use to service debt) at Rs.$2.0 billion. Even if this valuation prevails, the state's
total debt will grow to Rs.$13 billion.
3.            The interest rate on the refinanced debt will range from six percent to 7.5 percent
depending on the ultimate valuation of the assets transferred by the state. Even at 7.5 percent, this
represents a substantial drop in the state's average cost of funds. In terms of its impact on debt service,
however, it is not large enough to offset the growth in the stock of debt due to the incorporation of
BANERJ liabilities.
4.            Debt service on the refinanced debt, along with debt service from earlier reschedulings,
will be subject to a debt service cap of 13 percent of net recurrent revenues. Due to transitional clauses
in the debt agreement, the effective debt ceiling in 1997 will be about ten percent of revenues, rising to
11.5 percent of revenues in 1998. The stock of debt is therefore expected to grow, due to the
capitalization of debt service above the 13 percent ceiling. At the same time, arrears are expected to
increase. Even with the 13 percent ceiling, the state will be required to increase its cash outlays for debt
service. Given the rigidity of its other spending obligations, the state is likely to finance this through
increased arrears.
5.            The project will also monitor the state's investment program and fiscal performance
during the! disbursement period. This is not designed to induce improvement in the state's fiscal
situation. As noted earlier, the inability of the federal-state debt negotiations to produce a sustainable
workout6 renders the objective of state fiscal recovery temporarily nmoot. The intent of fiscal monitoring
e As seen in Table I OB-2, due to the model design, the debt:revenue ratio for 1996 already reflects the state-federal governments
debt deal concluded on January 15,1997 (i.e., the 1996 debt:revenue ratio is calculated as the stock of debt as of December 31,
1996, divided by revenues in 1996.

is merely to permit the Bank to halt disbursements of project funds in the event that the state engages in
reckless fiscal behavior.
