The state's compliance with this program will be a condition of disbursement for the time slice of
January-June 1998 and for the time slice related to the water and sanitation reforms.
7.             The second set of fiscal indicators refers to the state's overall fiscal performance. While
little improvement in the state's fiscal situation is expected, this provision will permit the Bank to halt
disbursements if the state engages in actions that result in substantial fiscal deterioration. To implement
this provision, the Bank and the government have agreed on a 'most likely" fiscal prognosis for the
state, based on a detailed examination of the factors that will affect each of the major items of receipts
and expenditures: GDP growth, tax effort, the number and salaries of personnel, investment spending,
and likely receipts from privatization and new borrowing. These have been incorporated into a detailed
fiscal projection for 1997. To facilitate supervision, the Bank and the government have agreed to use
two summary indicators as measures of fiscal performance:  the operational surplus (deficit)7 as a
percent of revenue, and the ratio of the stock of debte to revenue.  Based on fairly optimistic
assumptions--e.g., that (a) GDP will grow 4 percent; (b) tax effort will increase 3 percent; (c) total
personnel costs will drop three percent; and (d) investment spending will be largely limited to the major
projects cited in table 3.1--the target for the operational deficit in 1997 was fixed at 35 percent of net
revenues. The target for the debt stock:revenue measure was fixed at 2.85:1.
8.             To place these results in perspective, the fiscal projection was extended through the year
2002. As shown in Table IOB-2, the fiscal outlook for the state is unpromising. While the state would
achieve a primary surplus in 1999, debt service on contractual debt would remain high--at about 25
percent of revenues. As a result, the operational deficit would decline only slowly, reaching -12 percent
of revenues in 2002. As much of state's debt service would be deferred under the debt service ceiling,
the resulting accumulation of deferred interest would slow the rate of decline in the stock of contractual
debt. In 2002, the stock of contractual debt would be 224 percent of net revenues.
9.             Arrears are also expected to grow. The combination of stubbomly high personnel costs
and undeferrable debt service produced a cash flow deficit equal to five percent of revenues in 1996.
Projected growth in investment spending in 1997 and 1998 wili widen the gap. The accumulation of
arrears (including the capitalization of interest on arrears) is projected to produce a total debt stock equal
to 291 percent of revenue in 2002.
10.           It should be emphasized that projections for the outer years are extremely speculative.
It is particularly likely that the state will be forced to find a means to balance receipts and expenditures
7   The operational surplus (deficit) is defined as net current revenues, less the sum of (a) accrued interest obligations (including

8   Debt includes contractual debt (including debt refinanced under the recent debt agreement) and any net increase in arrears from

adjustments in debt service obligations to the federal government. The feasibility of such tactics will
depend upon changes in the Federal Constitution which are still under debate.

BRAZIL
RIO DE JANEIRO MASS TRANSIT PROJECT
ANNEX 11
PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION SCHEDULE, MONITORING, EVALUATION
AND SUPERVISION PLAN
A. Project Implementation Schedule
1.           The implementation schedule for individual components are shown in Annex 8. Those
schedules and the detailed cost tables of Annex 8 provide the basis for project monitoring and for the
disbursement profile.
2.           Implementation Monitoring. Flumitrens will monitor compliance with the individual
implementation schedules by defining target dates for key actions. The target dates for key actions for
the first two years of the project (to about mid-1997) are shown in the Action Plan in this Annex. In
November of each year Flumitrens will prepare, with the agreement of the Bank, a similar action plan for
the following year of implementation, and so on throughout the life of the project. In the event of delays,
Flumitrens will be responsible for identification of the constraints and for devising and executing policies
to resolve problems.
3.           Financial progress will be monitored by Flumitrens in the same manner as
implementation progress. Estimates of costs of Part A have been made but final costs will be dependent
on consultants' final engineering designs and on bid prices (for equipment etc.). Thus, it will be the
responsibility of Flumitrens to update cost schedules as contracts and bids are finalized and to monitor
financial progress in relation to the updated schedules.
4.           Flumitrens will report on both implementation and financial progress in the biannual
progress reports.
S.           Evaluation of Part A of the project will attempt to determine the extent to which original
construction schedules and costs are respected.
B. Monitoring and Evaluation of Project Components
6.           Monitoring would identify the physical, institutional and financial status of each
subproject component, as appropriate, on a periodic basis. The aim would be to identify implementation
problems and the extent of deviation from the targets proposed at appraisal. The results of the
monitoring program (the comparison of achieved performance with appraisal targets) will be presented in
the biannual Progress Reports prepared and coordinated by Flumitrens. The results of each
subcomponent monitoring program will provide the basis and information for implementing agencies to
prepare measures and actions to resolve implementation problems, if any, and at the same time, to
update project costs, disbursement and implementation schedules if necessary. Monitoring indicators to
be employed will vary by type of component but the general guidelines are set out in the following
paragraphs.
7.           The general guidelines given below would be followed:
(a)   each implementing agency would prepare, as part of its component preparation
report, an implementation schedule which sets out target dates for key actions
for physical works, equipment supply and institutional components. Where
components are not fully prepared at appraisal, it would be a condition of their
subsequent inclusion that an implementation schedule be prepared as an
integral part of the component;



-108                                ANNEX 11
(b)   for physical works (and equipment) progress the implementation schedule will
define target dates (to the nearest quarter) for the start and finish of key actions
involved in the implementation of physical works (and supply of equipment).
Thus indicators are likely to include:
(i)    start/finish dates for final engineering design and documents for works
(or equipment);
(ii)    date of issue of bid documents;
(iii)   date of contract signature; and
(iv)   start/finish dates for works (or supply of equipment).
(c)   for financial performance since works (or supply of equipment) are likely to
extend over some time, the component implementation schedule will also be
required to give target "percentage completed" by quarter. Thus target
expenditures can be estimated and will be used to monitor financial
(disbursement) performance on a quarterly basis;
(d)   for institutional measures (technical assistance, training and studies), the
monitoring targets will be the progress reports and the dates of completion
indicated in the implementation plan of Annex 7; and
(e)    monitoring of property values in the area of influence of the Flumitrens before,
during and after construction.
8.           Ex-post evaluation would be aimed at determining the effectiveness and efficiency of
measures in meeting the objectives determined at appraisal. In the event that objectives are not met and
previously anticipated improvements in performance are not achieved, the data would be used: (a) to
identify wvhy components had not been fully successful; and (b) to modify future components proposed
for subsequent implementation which were based on similar premises. Furthermore, the ex-post
evaluatic,n would be used as a major input to the Project Implementation Report.
9.           Quantitative performance targets have been established for Flumitrens and are shown
below.

10.          The qualitative monitoring indicators will be: (a) the date of the establishment of the
Regional Transport Coordination Commission as compared to the agreed date in Section IV of the main
text and its effectiveness in meeting the basic objectives described in Annex 3; (b) date of submission of
a financing mechanisms draft law to the SRJ legislature and an action plan to carry out its



-109                                  ANNEX 11
recommendations which should take place before the Mid-term Project Review; (c) the pace of the
implementation of the intermunicipal bus integration plan after with Flumitrens measured by the
additional number of lines integrated at stations; and (d) the results of a train user survey, to be carried
out every two years after effectiveness until project completion, by an independent firm, to gauge the
satisfaction of the users with the level of service offered by the system. All the above qualitative
monitoring indicators would be rated on a scale of 0 (unsatisfactory) to 5 (excellent).
11.           Bank Supervision. The main emphasis during the initial project phase would be to: (a)
review the development and implementation of the project monitoring system; (b) make certain that
Flumitrens' staff is familiar with Bank procurement guidelines, Bank disbursement procedures (i.e.,
Special Account transactions), and Bank independent auditing requirements; (c) ensure that all steps
towards decentralization of Flumitrens to the State are being taken; and (d) help Flumitrens/AMTU's
development of a strategy to properly promote the project to the RJMR public. Many of these items
would be addressed in the first supervision mission scheduled for January 1997. During project
implementation, the supervision missions would need to conduct the following activities in the field on a
regular basis in addition to monitoring project progress: (a) review a sampling of contract bids and
awards; (b) inspect disbursement procedures and record-keeping; (c) inspect adequacy of supervision
and monitoring of the implementing agencies and respective subprojects; (d) review performance and
accuracy of the Project Monitoring System; and (e) complete a Mid-term Review 24 months after project
effectiveness to examine the progress made, especially in the Integrated Land Use, Urban Transport,
and Air Quality Strategy and policies to assess the relevance of project objectives and actions, and
recommend appropriate revisions (as needed). Supervision missions in the field should attempt to visit
all the agencies dealing with the project during each trip. A preliminary schedule of supervision missions
including key activities is indicated in the table on the following page. The key activities shown are in
addition to the regular supervision needs, which include a review of: (a) progress reports; (b)
procurement issues; (c) disbursements; (d) Project Implementation Plan; (e) project costs; (f) yearly
independent audits; (g) correspondence; and (h) unforeseen issues.
12.          The Bank project team  would consist of the Task Manager (Sr. Transport Plan-
ner/Economist), a Railway Engineer and a Financial Analyst. Consultants are anticipated to be required
on a short-term basis to assist in specific areas, such as analysis of monitoring system, and review of
institutional development programs. Supervision requirements for the project are expected to be high
due to: (a) the complexity of the institutional arrangements; (b) the anticipated large number of small-
scale subprojects; and (c) the institutional weakness of Flumitrens (requiring significant training and
technical assistance). Supervision requirements are anticipated to be about 20 staff-weeks during the
first year and about 12-15 staff-weeks thereafter. A summary of key inputs is provided on the following
page.
13.           Borrower's Contribution to Supervision. Flumitrens is expected to: (a) be responsible
for project monitoring and coordination, to keep the Project Monitoring System accurate, and to follow-up
and address all issues which are "signaled" by the system; (b) be responsible for properly managing all
procurement and disbursement considerations, keeping procurement and disbursement records up to
date; (c) prepare progress reports in June and December of each year; and (d) be responsible for
coordinating arrangements for the Bank supervision missions.

BRAZIL
RIO DE JANEIRO MASS TRANSIT PROJECT
ANNEX 12
THE RJMR'S SOCIAL, ECONOMICAL AND URBAN TRANSPORT CHARACTERISTICS
A. General Aspects
1.             Urban population growth in Brazil has been impressive over the past several decades.
In 1940, the urban population was about 16 percent of the total population, increasing to 65.57 percent in
1980 and to 75.60 percent in 1991. According to the Constitution, States are allowed to designate
metropolitan, micro-regions and urban conglomerates. The metropolitan regions have been organized by
grouping neighboring municipalities, in order to integrate the planning and execution of public services of
common interest. Among all the metropolitan regions, RJMR stands out from the other, because it
comprises 77 percent of the population of the State, in an area which is equivalent to 15 percent of the
State's area, and therefore, an extremely high demographic concentration.
2.             Its urbanization process took place along natural vectors, most of them without basic
infrastructure, in view of the permissive laws of land use, especially in the Baixada Fluminense. The
region's topography induced the development vectors in the same directions of the mass transport
corridors, and these, in view of the investment policy, caused an increase or decrease of those vector's
development.
B. Socio-Economical Data
- Produce about 85 percent of the income of the State of Rio de Janeiro;
- Generates 4,500,000 jobs;
* Development indicators: 1980- 1990:
- energy consumption:   52 percent residential;
43 percent commercial;
74 percent industrial;
- jobs: 22 percent; and
- The Rio de Janeiro municipality has 56 percent of the population and 77 percent of the income of the
RJMR.

C. Transportation System
3.                The RJMR generates about 13.18 million trips/day, 48 percent from home to work. Of
the total trips, 67 percent are by public transport, 11 percent are by auto, 20 percent are by foot and the
remaining 2 percent by other modes. The public transport trips presents the following modal split.
Figure 12.1: RJMR Daily Trips (Work Day)
Modal Split Motorized Transpiort
Ferry Boat 1.0%
Car (private, taxi) 14.5%    Other 0.5%
Train 4.0%
Subw ay 3.0%
Bus 77.0%/
Source: PTM194 Total Daily Trips   1318 mnillion



-125 -                                 ANNEX 12
4.             Base on the research carried out for the Mass Transportation Plan of the RJMR (PTM -
1994), there is a very unbalanced modal split, with the buses playing the role of a structural
transportation mode, causing undesirable effects to the transportation system and therefore to the quality
of life of the region. In this context, the railway system operated by Flumitrens, presents all the potential
conditions for restructuring the transportation system, being present in 15 of the 17 municipalities that
form the RJMR, constituting a fundamental mass transport alternative, and linking all those 15
municipalities to the central area of Rio de Janeiro.
D. Flumitrens' Key Data
5.             Flumitrens' network, with an extension of 264 km (163 km electrified and in broad
gauge -1.60m, 9 km non-electrified in broad gauge, and 92 km non-electrified in 1.00m gauge), has five
main broad gauge electrified corridors: Deodoro, Japeri, Santa Cruz, Leopoldina and Belford Roxo; and
three corridors non-electrified, in metric gauge: Vila Inhomirim, Guapimirim and Niter6i.
6.             Nowadays, the daily transport is about 400,000 passengers, 95 percent in the broad
gauge system. There are 1,019 diary departures programmed, but only 80 percent of these trips are
carried out. In 1984 and 1985, about 942,000 passengers transported by working day, and taking into
account the income evasion, the total transport in working days was at that time about 1,200,000
passengers.
